Chapter LIII. Of the treatment of head wounds without fracture of the bone.
I have already said in the division of the treatment of wounds which concerned the book, and I say here the cure of these with the hand, and with the help of doctors according to the second aid.
I say, therefore, that wounds differ according to the cause of their being, and according to the place on which they fall.
Therefore the wounds that are often caused, are like shots of stones: or cuts of a knife, or a blow with a lance, or a piece of wood, or an arrow: and similar things from many other things.
But wounds that are caused according to the places of the body are, such as a wound that falls on the head, or the neck, or the chest, or the belly, or an organ: and similar ones from the members.
And I am telling the treatment of certain wounds, that I may set forth the reasoning and rule for the remaining wounds.
And I shall begin with simple head wounds: because the memory of the teacher will come at the beginning of Chapter III.
I say, therefore: When it comes to a simple wound on the head, and there is no fracture of the bone: then consider if it is from a shot of stone, or similar, and has broken the skin only: and it is a severe wound, and fear about the outcome of blistering fever: then make an immediate phlebotomy on the head: and do not delay that: and draw out his blood according to the quantity of its virtue: if there is no prohibition from phlebotomy, especially if it is the blood that flows out from the wound a little. And let the bleeding come from the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the bleeding come from the opposite side of the wounded part. Let the sick person avoid food, wine, and heavy meals.
But if there is a need to restrain something, then let it be restrained from food, and not approach meat or wine.
And place over the wound if there is a feverish condition, hot poultice with infused oil in rose water alone, or with wine where there is a stenosis.
But if you are free from a feverish condition, then place over the wound if it is new with its own blood, and has not changed its air, the powder which I will describe after a little while: and if it has already changed its air, then place over it some ointment, until it is healed.
But if the wound is large, or is from an incision of a sword, and similar: and its lips are not gathered by poultices, then gather it with a stitch, according to what I will describe in wounds of the leg about their stitches.
But if the wound has already been detected from a bone, and hangs, and there is no retention of it, except in a small suspender: then incision that wound. Then care for the wound with medicines, and restore it to its place of tough skin, and order the sick person to eat food in which there is a fermentation, such as heads of animals and cooked wheat, until it becomes like a pulse: and let them eat similar things.
That if the wound is in the skin and does not adhere to the bone: then incision all that part which is putrefied, and throw it away: and then care for it.
But if there is an artery in these wounds, or a new one from which blood flows: and does not become separated by medicines, then inquire about the artery. But if you do not cut that wound, then dry it up with Spike-root, or bind it up. That if necessity forces you to do this before what I have said is done, then burn it, until blood stops flowing from it.
If, however, these wounds are small and simple, then the matter is easy and light: so that the powder applied to them can be easily absorbed: because it solidifies before it changes by air.
The method of the powder for healing wounds. Take a part of olibanum, and two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of non-extinguished limestone: all are to be well mixed together, and sieved: then fill it with the powder, and bind it firmly in place until it sticks well to itself and congeals with blood.
Then tie the powder above it, and leave it, and do not remove it until it has remained in place: so that it does not crumble. For the medicine will not be removed from it until it is healed.
And when this lime is made alone by itself, when you do not have on hand olive oil either, and dragon' s blood also makes the same operation in many large wounds when it is put on them and bound together, and are recent wounds with their own blood, which has not been changed by air.
That if the air has already somewhat altered it, especially when it is in the summer season, then the intention of this powder is not in place: so take care to put on it some digestible ointment that we have mentioned, or put on it a paste made from barley flour, prepared with water and honey, until pus is discharged. Then take care of the rest with the usual cure, until they are healed.
If, however, there is a broken fracture of a bone and it is small, then extract it with the help of a hook: for I will now speak about the treatment of fractures of the skull.
Regarding wounds in the neck area.
If a wound has occurred in the neck, then there is no difference in treatment between it and a wound of the head, when the latter is simple.
But if the incision has already reached the nerve of the neck or an artery: then if it is a nerve, there is no skill in it, unless treatment with it causes a harsh constriction, like the cinchona and other such things. For they harm the nerves, and turn them back.
And do not put any cold thing on a wound at all: for the substance of the nerves is cold, and their extension is with the brain, which is an exalted member of the body: and let such a one be cured with medicines of softness, as the lime, when it is poured out with sweet water sometimes infused with oil, or with rosin oil, and turpentine, when it is washed also with sweet water: and other mineral stones, in this fashion, and humid unguents.
But if the wound is large, then administer a stitch: or cover its lips with dust. Then take care of yourself, until you are healed.
But if the wound is deep, or has occurred a pocket of pus in its lower part, then pierce it in a more depressed place.
That if the incision has already reached the nerve of an artery, and the flow of its blood is followed: then separate it, or dry it up, and bind it: or burn it, if necessity compels to do so.
But if the incision has already reached the spine's joint, or all of it, and so on as if it were a horse's joint: then join the skin's lips together with a stitch over the joint, and do not touch the joint, but rather make it fit the natural form. Then tighten it with a tightening that decreases gradually, and leave it for a few days. Then take care of yourself with unguents that cause dryness and healing.
But if the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then examine it: then if there are any fragments of bone, remove them all, or as many as you can: and what is not prepared, leave it, until the wound decays, and separates and discharges its excrement.
If the wound is fresh, then dust the powder on top of the powder that I have laid down. But if it is a case of a broken bone and a fistula: then take care of it with the Remedy that comes in the chapter about it.
On Wounds in Spatulas.
If the wound is caused by a lance or a knife, and you can see its depth, then examine it carefully. But if there is a flow of pus from the wound, and the patient groans because of it, then know that the wound is mortal.
But if it is not deep and is fresh with its blood: then do not put the powder on it at first hour, nor bind it up so that the blood does not hold in its depth and returns to the heart and makes him sick; but put on it a attracting ointment and undo it twice a day.
But if you do not have the ointment present, then put on the wound a used cloth, so that it sucks out what comes from it by moisture: and let the patient sleep on the wound so that what is gathered in it runs out.
But if three or more days have passed since the wound and there has been no spasms, no bad tremor of the heart, no tightness in breathing, and the other signs of it are good, then know that the wound is healed. Take care of it and with ease, and with the rest of the cure, until it is healed.
But if the healing of it is slow and it opens again always, then know that it has already become a fistula. Take care of it from the chapter about it.
And if the bone is from an incision of a sword or a knife, and it is simple on the surface of the back, or the back of the neck: then take care of the wound with the Remedy that preceded it from the sutura, if it is large: or with powder, if it is small.
If, however, he has already made an impression on the bone and has struck out from it a piece of fracture: then inquire about the rib, and approach towards extracting those fractures if they are separate. If, however, they are not separated, then let them go until the rib has putrefied, for the extraction of these will be облегчена.
But as regards the remains of injuries and accidents in the remaining members, it is a question of judgment in the treatment, which I have mentioned.
Regarding wounds in the belly and the exit of the intestine and the exit of them.
The rupture that has occurred in the belly is great, medium, and small.
So then when and how often the intestine emerges, or the number of intestines.
Why do they get mixed up, and their sewing is more painful and difficult?
And the small rupture also is difficult from another mode. So then, in order that the reduction of them may be more difficult, if you do not hurry up to bring it back to its place: for it swells, opens, and thickens: why does entry into it become difficult?
Therefore the middle disruption is better than the small one: for since the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult to reduce these two species.
Know that the縫合 of the belly is according to four ways.
The two common sutures meet in the Suture of the belly and in the Suture of the remaining wounds of the body: and the other two particular sutures communicate to the rest of the wounds. However, they are more proper when it is a matter of the Suture of the belly: and I will tell you one thing at a time, with an explanation.
I say, that when the rib is small and has come out a little of the intestine, and it is difficult to reduce it: then it is on account of one or both of these two ways. Or because of the swelling due to cold air in the intestine, as we have said:
When then is this so? Then it is necessary to warm him up, so as to submerge a sponge or a wet cloth in warm water. Or you cook it in squirted water and cypress and garlic and simmer with it the intestines until the swelling is resolved.
When then does this wine which has fever make? And it is better than water alone in resolving the swelling.
When then do you see that the swelling has already been resolved, then wet the intestines with the water in which it was cooked, or dry them. For by this his entry will be eased with the small object.
But if his return is excused after this treatment, then find in the disruption a little, with the instrument with which they cut the hemorrhoids.
And the first cure is better when prepared, than the incision: and it does not require a force to cut after need and want of instruments.
Let one part of it be acute and the other obtuse, and the extremities be a little sharp, rather blunt; and this is like a cauda, as you see it.
When then does the knot grow larger and enters into the intestine, then it is necessary that there be a return of him according to his natural form in his own place, if possible above it: for it is better.
But when the rupture is wide in the lower part of the groin: then it is necessary that you let lie the sick one on his back, and set his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the groin, then set his head and his chest higher than his lower parts.
And also if the rupture is on one side of the groin, then set your intention and your attention always in this, that part in which is the knot be higher on the other side.
And thus it should be that you administer this to large wounds and to medium-sized wounds.
But regarding small wounds, place it as you have prepared it for yourself.
Then make it present between the hands of a pious minister who holds the whole laceration in his hand: and spread out its lips. Then find someone who follows who has some thread; and this is the way of a common single stitch.
And there are those who take as much as you want, or count as much as you want according to the width of the wound. Then let go from the end of the laceration by the amount of your finger, that is, the doctor: and fasten now one after you have inserted it in at both ends of the skin, with two ends of the thread that are under the skin from within, until you have made it penetrate from the other side, and you have already joined together the two ends of the skin, and the two ends of the thread: and there are four orders.
Then tighten with double thread around the needle from both sides at the same time, until the two lips of the wound have been brought together by the decreasing gathering.
Then let go of the thickness of the finger as well, and fasten in them another: then make it straight with thread, just as you did with the needle first time. So you should not stop doing this with him as you go along from the beginning until you have completed the compression of the whole wound.
And there are medium needles between the thickness and the fineness: because the fine ones cut flesh quickly; and the thick ones enter difficult into the skin: therefore it is necessary that they be both of a middle size, and of a fineness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be fastened in both ends of the skin close together: so that the flesh does not quickly cut; and the wound does not open before its consolidation, and it does not elongate with the stitch, so that it does not prevent the wound from being consolidated.
Then cut the end of the acute edge so that it does not hurt the sick person in his sleep; and put on them little balls made from thin cloths from all parts that hold the ends of the sharp edges; and let them go until you know that the injury has already healed.
And this type of stitch with acute points is much more convenient for small wounds. Since it is sufficient in the stitch of their one, or two, or around them.
But the second common stitch is to collect with the stitch the four ends: the ends of the two skins, and the ends of the two sfac in one thread, with an acute point in subtlety and thickness.
Then when you penetrate with the acute point all four ends, draw them back from the part from which you started in order for the thread to come out from the upper part of the wound: so that the stitch is, like a stitch on cloth when cloth is stretched, and place between every stitch the amount of thickness of the little finger.
This stitch everyone knows.
And with this stitch I sewed a wound that occurred to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the injury to the flesh was large with a palm's width: and it came out from the intestines about two palms from the middle intestine, and there was an injury in the middle of the stomach.
So I reduced that after he had stood for twenty hours.
The wound was healed in a space that had been for about fifteen days: and I took care of it, and he was cured: and after that he lived for many years, thriving in his affairs.
And the doctors judged that it would not heal.
But by a miracle I did not cure that with ointment. Since I was in a place where the ointment was not found, and there was no medicine. So I put on the wound a cotton cloth wetted with honey twice a day, and took care of its washing with honey water until it was cured.
Two kinds of stitches I shall call by their own names according to the speech.
And it is as follows, beginning with an acute stitch: and insert it from the outside to the inside part.
When the needle enters the skin, and runs through the length and width of the whole body: let go of the end of the thread where you inserted the needle, and let it penetrate another end from the inside to the outside in an opposite direction.
When you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate again at this same end from the outside to the inside: and let go of the end of the thread that is in this side, and make it penetrate another end from the inside to the outside - opposite direction - from this same end: and when you make it penetrate, then make it penetrate by your penetration, which makes it penetrate in this end, that is on the opposite side, until it penetrates all of it.
Then also from this same side, and take it by the end that is on the opposite side, and bring out the needle where it penetrated the skin.
Then also reduce it in that skin, and take its end that is on the opposite side, and bring it together with this end where you are; and bring out from the skin that which was brought out from the opposite side: and make that one go out before the other - as many times as necessary - until they are completely outside according to that example.
And the upper part of this stitch is as follows; it is that their stitches should be close together, like those that make the skin smooth: so that its end of the thread is brought out from one side of the skin; and let go of it on the other side until you have finished it.
The second kind of stitch belongs to Galen.
This speech of his has been told, that their stitches should be made similar in such a way as some doctors make them, so that they gather together all the parts by their natural shape: and they bring the end of the thread to one another, and this end where it is brought together with its opposite. And this I will tell you next.
You should make the incision at this point, close to the outer edge near you, and make it penetrate into the inside, in that same place only: and you shall leave two ends of the incision, rifacendo. Then turn the incision around, and make it penetrate from the outside to the inside at both ends, that is to say on each side: rifacendo.
Then turn both sides around, and make them penetrate from the inside to the outside at both ends, that is to say on each side opposite: rifacendo.
And this method of stitching is better than the common easy stitch, and it is a stitch which has a memory: the one before it.
This is thus the speech of Galen also.
And certain ones said. The stitch of the hand with two needles. That is to say, that you should put into them both one wire: and begin with the inserting of the needle, since it is with you, and make it penetrate to the other side, from one side to another, in accordance with the stitches of those which are equal.
And you should know, that when the break is in the middle of the hand: then the stitch of it is more difficult than at any other part of the hand.
Judgment indeed of the treatment of wounds, is judgment of all wounds.
That is to say, because when you have made the wound clean with its own blood, before it cools itself: and you have reduced the entrails, and they themselves, and made them fit: then put on the poultice of scar.
That is to say, if the air has already altered the wound: then put on the poultice of some ointment which is near you, until it makes the pus: and let the wires drop off, and be firm on each side, and the incision close up. Then take care for that wound as is taken care of all wounds, until it heals.
If you have not the medication for that wound, then put on it immediately, so that it may begin to emit pus, and change the poultice twice a day, as I taught you, until it heals.
But if there are no other conditions, and the wounds are simple, then there is no need for medication.
If, however, you should come to the conclusion that there is an injury to the main member of the body, then it is necessary to immerse the wound in a cloth dipped in oil of moderate temperature, or in rosin oil: and place it around the places that are between the hips, and under the armpits.
That if it feels pain, or prickings, or putrefaction in its intestines (for this often happens), then cleanse the bowels with astringent wine, black, hot: especially if putrefaction has already reached the intestines and a penetrating gangrene has taken hold up to the peritoneum.
And know that what is from the large intestine is easier to heal: and that which is from the small intestine is more difficult to heal.
But the intestine which is called the jejunum does not accept healing from a wound that has occurred in it at all: and this is because of its multitude, since it is made up of loops, and because of its size, and because of its softness, and because of the closeness of its nerves.
But if the wound that has come from it is not deep, and you are able to grasp it well: reduce it according to your own judgment, reducing the intestines equally.
But if there is a space that has been passed through it, and it has already become bright or black: then you should tighten it with a thread above the place that has been discolored by it, so that blood does not flow. For in the loop there are arteries and veins.
Then cut what is beyond that ligament, and place two ends of a thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside the body: so as to relieve its pull on you, and to extract it toward the opening of the loop, and to emit the pus of the wound.
Remembrance of the wound that happened in the Intestine.
When disruption occurs in the intestine and is slight: then it is possible to restore it in some people; because I saw one who was wounded in his abdomen with a lance, and there was an old wound on the right side of his stomach, and it had healed up; and a fistula was made, and came out of it belching and wind.
I therefore applied the treatment, until I was confident in his recovery; and I did not cease to examine him carefully, until he was well and the site had been strengthened.
Once I saw the place was now consolidated, I feared for the disease that might come upon him in his abdomen: and it did not happen to him from that accident any evil. And he remained in better health, eating and drinking, and enjoying himself, and entering a bath, and exercising his body.
And some experimenters said, for when disruption occurs in the intestine and is slight: then it should be tied according to this method. And it is, that you take ants of large heads. Then gather two lips of the wound, and put one ant on them which has an open mouth over the two lips of the wound. Then when the head is on it, and it tightens its mouth, cut off the head; for it clings to it, and does not come off.
Then put another ant next to the first, and do not stop doing this with the ant after the second amount of wound.
Then reduce the wound, and tie up the wound. Those heads remain annexed to the intestine, until it is fed by the intestine, and is healed, and does not happen to the sick person any evil inside.
And it is possible that the intestine be tied also with a thin thread; which is taken from the animal's intestine, annexed to it after it enters into the nest.
And it is, that you take the end of this thread out of the intestine, and cut off a lot of it. Then tie his filament with a thin silk thread. Then insert that filament into the cavity, and sew it to the intestine. Then reduce the wound.
And this method of sewing with the awl and intestine is no more than a second opinion of trust and hope.
You know, a wound that is large and broad, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines; then it is not a skill but a delicate operation, and its healing is not at all certain.
Regarding the care for the small intestine and fistulas.
You should know that every wound, or suppurating sore when it has healed over and become old, and has formed a ulcer, and still discharges pus always, and does not heal: then we call it universally a fistula wherever it may be in the body; and we name it Zuchemen itself.
And a fistula is, in fact, a kind of filiation according to the nature of the part affected; it is hard and white: there is no pain with it, but there are curly feathers: and because of this it is called by some people Risati, that is, feather-like.
And it is sometimes moist, discharging pus. And perhaps it heals by granulation in some hours. When this moisture is much, and there is little: and when it is thick, and when it is thin.
I have already پیش in the division, then when I said wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal, and does not form new flesh, is not a wound at all but a fistula for nine causes.
One of these causes is, or because there is a lack of blood in the body; or because of its malicious nature; or because it is between itself and over its lips hard flesh, which prevents the growth of new flesh; or because it is full of dirt and filth; or because the wound is putrid within itself, and the matter which it discharges is of bad quality; or because medicine is incompatible in its cure; or because corruption by falling into a region is a kind of pestilence, or per se in that region: for this reason digestion of ailments is difficult, and healing of ulcers by nature is delayed; or because there is only one opening, or a number of bones.
And I have already shown you all the causes and signs of them, and their treatments with physicians in detail; and it is necessary here that I make you know the treatment of the fistula and of thezuken, which is made by a surgical opening with the hand.
So you know that the fistula happens in all parts of the body. For these are the fistulas that reach the large veins and arteries, or nerves, or to the intestines, or to the urethra, or to some of the ribs on the back, or to some of the joints of the body by composition: like the hands and feet: and those that flow close to the main member, and others.
Now which of these fistulas is according to this way, it is from diseases that are difficult to cure, and does not accept treatment, and the work with her is labor and ignorance.
That if there is any fistula whose depth does not reach these places that I said: and you hope, and you trust in its cure, and you seek to come to its knowledge, then you accept a trial from bronze or iron if the fistula extends according to rectitude, and you search it with itself.
But if there is any fistula that has a tortuosity, then investigate it with a thin lead. For because the lead with its gentleness softens itself to the body, and it is concerned about the tortuosity.
That if the fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to indicate on it with a lead. So make a hole from its holes from its holes.
For because the moisture that clisterizes itself, runs towards other openings, and flows out of them.
Then investigate and find out according to how you can be able to: that you may know if there is a mouth or nerve, and the fistula is hidden far away or near: or is a single fistula whose openings are many, and it is over all that with great strength of your own: with your questioning, which you ask the sick person, and by finding out the pain when compressing with your hand on the spot, and similar indications and causes of accident.
Therefore, when you are standing still, stationary and stable, then turn to treatment with confidence: and it is as if you consider that, if there is a fistula that appears close by, or in a place far from the juncture, or from a nerve, or an artery, or a vein, or one of the places I told you, then dry up the fistula according to what I have previously said in my narrative, and remove what is in it from filtration, and腐敗した肉、それはその穴から生じるから、またはそれに似たもの: and cure it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and straight: then you should separate it in the depth as much as you can. Then cleanse it from all of its corrupted flesh.
Then apply the poultice of wet wool in acute medicines: and insert it into the fistula which you have enclosed with iron: do this several times, until the acute medicine has completely corroded what remained in the enclosure of the fistula from corruption.
Then restore it with ointment that generates healthy flesh, until it is healed.
But if it does not heal with this, then cauterize it according to what we have described.
But if the cause of the fistula is an opening, and this is certifed to you: then inquire about it, and dry it up according to what I have told you: if it does not prohibit you from any vein, or nerve, or main member, as we have said.
But if it is discovered to you that there is an opening, and there is some corruption, and blackening: then shave it, until all the corruption is removed. Then care for this person with what incarnates flesh, until he is healed.
That if it does not heal, and it discharges pus as it did: then know, that you have not achieved total eradication of the corruption.
Therefore, examine it again, and carefully consider whether there is some rational explanation for its condition, and cleanse it with your last strength.
Then restore him. So if he recovers, it is good; and if not, then you know that that corruption is far away in the depths of the body, which you have not reached. So you do not have such an art, unless you let it go by nature itself.
That if the corruption is in a small hole, and it is possible for you, draw it out with delicate forceps which are suitable for that.
If there are many bones, then inquire about their attraction, and do not let go of any of them with your strength.
If any of them make you afraid because there is a contrary moisture, and there is no fear or mistake in cutting them: then cut those moistures and the meat, if there is any, and free the bones.
If you do not have the preparation to remove the bones from your time, then place over the wound that it may rot: and let go of it until it rots what is around those bones of meat and moistures.
And guard the bone so that it does not harden again. And let it be constricted as you followed in your operation. At the bottom place over the wound, if you are able, a trodden-in with oil of sulfur: or a trodden-in in Egyptian ointment, or any kind of ointment.
For when those moistures rot, and the bones separate from them, their removal is eased, and their attraction is gone.
If there is one large bone, such as a thigh or hip bone, and that which is corrupted from it is part of the great bone, then cut it with the last rasp, until it is taken away by the black and corruption. Then restore the bone.
If, however, what is corrupted in it is a large part, and the corruption has already reached the bone's marrow, then you need to cut it off, and cut all of it up to the place where the corruption reaches: then take care of it, until it hardens again.
And I tell you a story about what happened to a man in his leg: that you may have an example and helper for your own operation.
There was a young man of about thirty years of age to whom it came to pass that he had pain in his leg, through some cause which entered into him from within the body, that is, from within, until it reached his limb; and there formed upon it a great ulcer, and not for any external cause.
So then with this time of error of the doctors, until the ulcer opened up, and there came many substances to him. And his cure was delayed until his leg had been bound up, and many openings were made in him: all of which were emitting pus, and moistures from the body.
So he was treated by a number of doctors for about two years, and there was no wise man in the art of hands, until I was called, and I saw his leg, and the matter which was flowing out from those openings in great quantity: also all of his body had become yellow, and his color was citron-like.
So then I entered into one of the openings, and came to the mouth.
Then I examined all the openings: and I found them coming together at a place from all parts of the leg.
So then I took hold of it, and followed up on one of the openings, until I detected a part of bone: and I found it corroded: which was already corroded, and discolored, and rotten, and pierced, until it penetrated all the way down to the marrow.
So then I broke off what had been detected by me, and prepared it from corrupt bone.
And I took hold of what was not in the bone but that corruption which I had broken off, and I made it sharp, and I broke it off. So then I had already roots.
Then I set about the root of the ulcer with cementing medicines in a long space. It is not consolidated.
And then I tried again, and grabbed the second bone above the first fracture: and I found continuous damage with the bone; and I tightened that which also seemed to me from that same corruption.
Then I tried to restore it. And it was not restored.
I grabbed it again, and I did not stop hitting the bone in detail, and tried to restore it: and it was not restored until I hit a little palm of the bone and pulled it out with its medulla.
Then I restored it with the help of physicians, and it was quickly consolidated, and healed: and this repetition in its operation, and section, would not have been necessary, except for the weakness of the nurse, and her lack of tolerance, and my fear of it from death; because it happened to him at all times from an excessive bad syncopis.
He was therefore completely healed: and new bone grew in place of the old one, and good order was restored in his body, and his strength returned: and he recovered his health, and returned to his habits: and he did not experience any harm at all during walking.
If however a prominent bone is already broken in the place of the body: then it should be fixed like this: you take a ligament, and tighten it on the end of the prominent bone: and order someone who will stretch it upwards: and put another grosser ligament on the first ligament. Then you fasten the ligament on top of the meat that is the bone: and extend its end so that you pull the meat downwards: and you cover the meat over the place that you want to fix, so as not to hurt it when you cut the meat.
Place a ligament, or a board under the bone below to the side: for when you do that, you should not stop to cut the corrupted meat.
And it should be fixed on the place of corruption slightly, so that there is not the risk of the bone's fracture: and it does not appear from its appearance to the sense, and compels to the force of its own fixing.
If the jaw is corrupted and is not prominent, then a part of it continues with another part: and corruption is in the middle of it, or in some part of it: then discover flesh from all parts altogether. Then put wood below.
Then tighten that from the first part where there is corruption until it continues with the lock from the other part: and tighten the second according to the length of corruption just a little, as I said.
If the corruption is in the joint, then cut that joint that is corrupted, and tighten the jaw where it continues from the other part.
But if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no skill in that except for abrasion.
If the corruption is in the hand or foot plank: then the thing in it is difficult to do very much. But it is necessary that you inquire how the corruption appears to you. And abrasion it, and make clean that according to what seems to you possible, and with whatever skill you can correct it: when it does not give you a contrary vein, or nerve.
And know that there are many saws and drills to cut these bones according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness and lightness, and their size and smoothness, and their magnitude and hardness, and their rarity. Therefore it is necessary that you prepare for each kind of operation a tool suitable to that operation.

Regarding the cutting of bones and the serrations on them.
The ends putrifiy, either because of an external cause, or for an internal one.
But when you have cured the disease with medicine, and there is no benefit from the cure; and you see corruption affect a limb, but it does not spread to any other part of the body; then you should cut off that limb all the way up to the place where the infection stops; so that the sick person may be saved from death or from a greater disease by means of this limb.
And the sign that this happened is that the limb is disfigured until it is healed; either it has burned itself up or rotted after yellowing; until this putrefaction comes to that part which follows the limb, and creeps all over the body.
Therefore, when it is incised like this, and similarly if the cause of corruption is from the beating of certain fruits: as in the case of the sea scorpion, and the wasp, and the nettle, and others like them.
If then corruption or a blow is in the extreme part of the finger, then cut the finger, and not leave room for corruption, so that it may be incised all the way to the remaining hand.
And likewise if it happens in the hand, then cut it from the root with fire, and not leave room for corruption, until it is taken in the arm of fire.
And if a wound has happened in it, then cut the arm at the hip where it joins.
But if corruption has passed over, and you see it all around the hip: then death is in that person. And to add to this cure, there is a quantity of virtue.
And likewise do for the foot, when a wound has happened in the finger, then cut it near one of the joints: and if it enters into the shank, then cut the whole foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then cut the leg at the knee joint.
Which if corruption has already reached above the knee, there is no remedy but to cut it off and let go of the sick one to death.
And the way to cut a limb or to bend it is, so that you stretch the ligament in the lower part, which you want to incise: and stretch another ligament above the place. And let one minister of the ligaments stretch to the inferiores: and let another minister of the ligaments stretch upwards to the superior. And you separate the flesh which is between the two ligaments, with a sharp instrument, until all the flesh is seen. Then it should be incised, or stretched.
And it is necessary that you place a linen cloth on all sides and not touch the sound place with the saw: and happen to the sick person a wound added, and a poultice hot.
If, however, a flow of blood occurs inside while you are working, then quickly cauterize the place: or put on it some powder that stops the blood. Then turn your attention to your own medication until it is complete.
Then bind the injured member with a suitable bandage and take care of him, until he recovers.
And I will tell you what happened to the man in his foot, this same accident that I am telling you.
That is, because it happened in his black foot, with a burn that resembles fire; and that corruption first occurred in his toe, until it began to affect the whole foot.
Then the man from his own fever, after he saw the corruption enter into his limb with its violence, which he felt in pain and burning; and because of that infection he cut himself where it joins, and was healed.
And after he had passed a long time, the same corruption happened to him again in the first joint of his hand.
So he came to me, and I tried to remove that excess with him when I put on the hand from the medicines after purifying his body: and the excess did not respond, and began to affect another finger: until it started to affect the hand.
So he called me to cut off his hand; and I did not want to do so, hoping for a response from within to that excess: and I was afraid also of causing death to him by cutting off his hand. For the strength of the man was above his destruction.
Then, when he was disappointed with me, he turned back to his land; and he came back to me, because he hoped for healing, and he cut off all his hand: and he was healed.
I did not bring this story forward unless it should serve as an aid to what concerns this kind of sickness; and so that it should make clear what is made clear, and in accordance with what is relevant.
On the treatment of ulcers and the quality of poultices with medicines.
When an aphma occurs in some fleshy member and the space of the aphma is prolonged until it forms a collection: then it bursts, or pierces, and what is contained within it comes out, leaving a hollow place like a wound, and the skin above it, as though it were a thin cloth; and does not spread to the last extent of corruption in itself, nor prints into the mouth, nor into the nerves, nor into the ligaments: it deserves to be called a sinus: yet if it prolongs the space, until it impresses corruption on some of these members, then it is called a fistula, or a pudenda.
And the care of it is that you should strike all the skin off as though it were burned up; and above all when it has become thin, and is like a cloth: and be confident that it would not stick to the place because of its own corruption.
If, however, you hope that the skin will adhere, and does not bring about the last corruption: and if there is a thickness of flesh on the skin, then care for it with poultices; and you are to consider in regard to a sinus.
Then if it is large, and the pus that is discharged from it has a foul odor: then poultice it with Egyptian medicine, which is acid, and oil, and myrrh, parts equal, blended together in water: and let them boil on the fire until the medicine turns red: and let it come to a syrup.
Then take from it what you need: and dissolve it with water and honey, and poultice it with the sinus: and tighten the opening of it, and let the medicine flow into it for two hours.
Then draw it out with expression: make those days until the sinuses are cleaned and the smell is gone.
And when it is sometimes clotted with blood and water of ash, when this remedy is not present with you: and it is, so that you take the ash of dried wood, or the ash of oak wood: and you pour over it water. Then wrap it up, and clatter it with it until you are confident that the sinuses have been washed away.
That if the tolerance of the Egyptian remedy is not good, and neither the water of ash, then clatter it with water and honey, to which has been added some crushed zircon. Or clatter it with honey and wine mixed, because the property of honey is to clean and wash away: and the wine makes it adhere to the sinuses, especially when there is an abundance of astringency and dryness in it.
When you do this occasionally, and are confident that the corruption of the sinuses has already been removed: then clatter it with it so that flesh is born in it.
And it is so that you take the dyophin from plaster: and dissolve it with rose oil, and wine astringent: or clatter it with some other remedies, of which I have written about their properties in the treatise on ointments.
That if the orifice of the sinus is tight, in which the cannula of the clister does not enter: then enlarge it with iron a little: or put in it the lyme-tree that is stirred in Egyptian ointment, or in basilic ointment until it is enlarged.
And likewise if the orifice is also wide, then join the lips of it with a stitch: and let go from it as much as is added to the clister without any addition.
And likewise if the orifice of it from which the pus runs is raised upward: then dry it out in a lower place where it is located, so that the pus runs from it downward: because when the pus is held back in the depth of the sinus, it prevents flesh from being born in it.
If it is not possible for you to cut around the bottom of the sinus, according to what you wish: then try to prepare the member in such a way that a pus comes out easily, according to how it prepares itself for you. Try not to leave anything behind in it at all: and take the solid medicines that are placed over the mark, fitting for what you wish. And extend them over the fabric of linen. And let there be such a quantity of cloth so that it communicates with all of the sinus. But the one who is on the sinus opening is the one whom you remove at all times, so that it comes out what is accumulated in it from pus: and know, that which runs out of it virus, is very much, or little: or digested, or undigested.
And with this repeatedly consider the place of the sinus itself, whether the master feels pain there, or not have pain in it, and whether it is in it a swelling, or not: and if it is in it a swelling, great or small; and if it is hard or soft.
When then your medicine is extended thus, and you see the material that comes out over the sinus opening, know that the sinus has already come together, or is approaching coming together: so then loosen it in the morning, or on the third day: and when you loosen the knot, then change the linen cloth, and rub with unguents.
If however comes out from the sinus after a long space a subtle virus, then do not despair about its coming together, for its cure approaches, because there are those who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and there are those who act in the opposite way.
If it has come out of the sinuses after many days of being indignant, then you will know that the sinus does not adhere.
If, then, the adhesion of the sinus is delayed, and its duration is prolonged: then add to the exiccation of medicines, it is true that physicians make them so that they are in their own substance of moisture, and in their own strength dry: like unguentum diacuminicon when added thereto an excess of addition by zeyi.

Regarding the cure of Panăritus and the grinding of a fingernail and incising a finger and cutting it off:
The finger nail grows from under the thumb of the hand or foot, and possibly also from the other fingers.
When it is then allowed to grow on and neglected with regard to its care, and is made an ulcer with heat: or if it is corrupted, and discharges pus, until it corroded the nail's root: and perhaps it corrupts all of it, and possibly the corruption reaches the mouth, until it has a foul smell: and makes the end of the finger large, and makes its color dark.
When then you shall care for it, with what concerns the above-mentioned, and your treatment does not benefit it: then you must cut away the whole superfluity that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For the caustic of the wound is very hot.
But if the mouth is healthy, and the nail also is healthy, and the nail's additions of thin blood press on the flesh towards the inside, and begin to sting it, and hurt it: then you should put a small radium under the edge of the nail that stings the flesh, and push it upwards: and cut the flesh easily, and put on what remains of the flesh from the medicines burning, and corroding, until it has all fallen away. Then care for that with the nail-oil, until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already imprinted itself into the mouth, then you should cut away that mouth, and take it out: for it is not healed at all while it remains in it.
If you see that the corruption and ulcer are running down the finger, then cut it off at one of its ends, according to what has been said above in regard to the reminder. Then care for the wound, until it is healed.
But if a nail is broken or struck: and there is sharp pain with it, then you should take away the blood first. Then dry the nail with a sharp spatula, cutting across from top to bottom.
And be careful so that the cut does not go down to the flesh that is under the nail, for then you will cause a very painful sore to arise, and it will be a reason for the birth of flesh in that place.
Then care for the place with the same ointment that soothes pain.
The finger that is added which is born in some hands of men, perhaps is fleshly all over; and perhaps there are bones in it, and perhaps a nail is born in it: and perhaps its birth is in the root of the joint, or its birth is in some nods of the finger. So then who is born in some nods of the finger does not move; and who is born next to a joint of the finger, perhaps moves.
But of that which is fleshly there is an easy way to cut it. That is, so that you strike yourself at the root with a sharp spatula.
But of that which its birth is in the root of the joint, there is a difficult treatment. So then abstain from cutting it.
But in that which is born in the finger up to the very nods, then it is necessary so that you strike the first flesh of its round incision all the way to the mouth. Then tighten the mouth with one of those sharp things that are suitable for it. Then care for the flesh so that it heals.
But the healing that happens to fingers from one side to another, often happens because it is born with man, that is, it is born with him; or from the healing of a wound, or from burning of fire, and similar things.
So then let there be separation of that healing so that fingers grow back to their natural form. Then place between them a leather strip, or rags soaked in rose oil: so that they do not quickly come together and separate them: or place between them a thin lead strip, so that the place heals as it should.
And likewise if some fingers with the palm are consolidated, then dry out that consolidation, according to how it is prepared and suits the figure of the member.
¶ On Incision.
Wounds are veins that are swollen, thick, and full of melancholic excesses which occur in many parts of the body; and most of them are found in the legs, especially in those who walk a lot or work hard, and those who carry heavy loads.
Therefore, you should provide for the discharge of the body from black cholera by a strong expiration sometimes.
Then take blood from the patient with a large blade.
The treatment of these wounds with iron is according to two methods. One is that they sit and black blood is drawn from them. And the other method is that a vein is lifted and all of it is drawn out.
But the sectioning thereof is according to this manner. Wrap the leg first with warm water until the thick, foul blood resolves. Then tie the leg of the patient by an nurse from the upper part of the thigh up to the lower part of the knee with a string. Then dry a vein in one place, or two, or three wide sections.
Then draw blood from the lower part of the leg up to the top and at the top it should be that the phlebotomist draws blood from the patient first. Then dry the nail down to the tip: until it exits with an amount of blood that you see sufficient to meet its strength and endurance.
Then tie it up: and command him not to abstain from foods that cause black cholera: and repeatedly draw blood and bleed when the veins are filled, and let the patient suffer.
But the lifting thereof is according to this manner. Cut the leg of the patient if there are many hairs. Then introduce yourself into the bath; or apply a warm damp cloth to his leg, until it turns red and the veins swell; or exercise him strongly, if there is no bath present, until the limb warms up.
Then dry the skin with a towel along the course of the vein; or at the heel of his leg, or at the lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with a hook, and scrape the vein from all sides until you feel it; and at its appearance, that is when you see it turn red from black to white. When therefore you remove the skin, you will see it white as a string.
Then introduce underneath it the hook with a blunt point, until it comes out and leaves the skin; hang it by the blade of the hook, soft.
Then dry another piece of skin next to that piece of skin with three fingers. Then scrape the skin from the upper part of the vein, until it appears. Then rub it with rays as you did before, and hang it by another hook as you did at first.
Then dry another piece of skin, or more pieces if you can endure it. Then rub it and cut it at the heel.
Then pull it out to the piece that is above it.
And do this, until you pull it out from the upper part; or higher parts of the skin, until it comes out all the way; then cut it.
If, however, it does not obey you in being pulled out and rubbed: then introduce a sharp wire into it, bind it, and pull it out; or introduce a hook underneath it: and rotate your hand around all its parts, until it comes out all the way. Then caustice, lest it cut. For if it cuts, it will be difficult for you to rub it well; and introduce yourself into the wound with a needle.
So when you have drawn it all out: then place it on the wound, soaked with wine and oil, or just oil, and care for it, until it is healed.
If the vine is contracted, and has twists to it which require stretching out towards the ends, and is not like what we have said according to its straightness: then you must find out over it to all parts of its twisting, and the places of its stretching out.
Then hang it up with hooks until you have completely stretched it out: and be careful when you cut your piece above it so that you do not hit a vein, or wound: for it would be difficult to stretch it out over you, or to draw it out. Therefore preserve your wits.
The shape of the incision when the vine is healed.
The shape of the incision when the vine is blind.
It is not a gentle curve like the rest of the vine, and it does not have a sharp extreme, so that it does not hurt the skin: and it has a coarse flexibility, soft: for if it were thin, it would bump into the skin with its thinness: but its coarse flexibility makes it suitable, as we have said.
Of the extraction of a vein from the leg.
This vein is born in the legs in warm countries, like the alnet, and the land of the Arabs, and in arid, warm lands, scarce of tenderness, that is, where no grass grows, nor trees.
And perhaps it is born in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from decay which happens under the skin, like what happens inside the bodies of snakes and worms, and worms are generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of this vein is that which happens in the leg: there is a violent inflammation. Then the spot becomes sore. Then the vein begins to come out from the spot of its sore, like a root of a plant, or an animal.
When this extreme end therefore appears from her, then you should fasten a lead screw on it, the weight of which is from a drachm to two drachms and a mark; and let go the lead that is suspended in the leg.
Whenever anything therefore comes out of her to the outside, fasten it in lead, and mark it. That if it is prolonged for a long time, then cut off a part of it, and fasten the remainder. And do not cut her off by the roots before she has come out all at once. For if you should cut it, it will contract, and go into the flesh; and it will cause a boil, and decay in the place, and a sore wound.
Therefore it should be handled gently, and taken out little by little, until it has come out from the last of its own; and nothing remains in the body from it: and from this vein comes out in some men who are five palms long and ten fingers wide. And already has come to me, for she has brought forth a son from twenty palms.
If however you should cut yourself in the time of your treatment: then drive in a lead screw into the hole, and pierce her through the length, with the body, until all that is in her comes out from that matter; and administer decay to the place with medicines for several days. Then take care of this cure for boils.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears in the ankle of the foot, or in the foot itself: and it has many openings, and comes out from every opening a branch.
Take care therefore for this cure, as we have said in the division, and with what follows that which has been mentioned.
Regarding the section on worms that are generated under the skin, and it is called Boona Disease.
This Disease is called in some country Bouina Disease; because it often happens to the worms, and it is a scaly one-sided skin: it is born between the skin and flesh.
And he felt all over the body, ascending and descending; and his sense was shown to him by his walking with a member from one part of the body to another, until he stopped wherever he should stop on the skin, and left it.
And his progeny is from the decay of some of the humors. As happen worms and snakes, that is, lombrics and roundworms in the belly.
And they fear his wickedness. For when he himself felt all over the body and went up to the head and came to a place: then perhaps it opens within him and leaves, and destroys his eye: and this happens many times.
But when you are to treat him, and take him out, then it is not done except by his walking and showing of his sense.
Therefore, it should be that you hold back what is above him, and what is under him with strong constriction by a ligament. Then dry up on top of him, and take him out.
That if it penetrates into the flesh, and not into his body: then put on the place a caustic with fire, until he dies.

On the division above infirmity of Nakir.
This infirmity is called in our land Nakir, and it is a pain that happens in some member; then it changes from one part of the body to another.
And I myself have already seen him, according as I tell you. I have been called to attend a sick woman in some trouble. And he felt the arm and saw the swelling of the lung in the rope of the forearm.
And after a while I saw one swelling rise with a feverish heat as if it were a wound, going up to his shoulder faster than he could run: so that it looked like silver running from place to place.
So the pain left that spot and was fixed in the shoulder.
Then he sat for a while, and then he ran all over his body until he reached the other arm.
Then he told me that he was shaking all over his body, according to which I saw it.
So I was amazed at the speed with which he moved from one limb to another. And I too had not seen this illness with my eyes before, moving in such a way. However, I saw many who suffered from one limb to another: and I did not see him with my eyes as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think that was because it is so, that he was from those who are afflicted with dry gangrene of the skin: hence this seems to indicate the windiness that is moved.
And it is necessary so that it does not appear in this way in those who are delicate and have moist bodies, hidden gangrene.
Then when you are treating his cure and the patient feels pain: then if it appears to you with your eye as we have said, then tie him down tightly on top of it and quickly dry him off under it until this windiness is driven away: and burn the spot.
But if you do not see the spot with your eyes: then take care of the body with the scrubbing and with it driving out the windiness, and dyeing them red with bitter herbs, and with herbs of serapina, and others similar to these.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows differ according to their kind and the places in which they enter the body.
Indeed there is a difference between them, since some have large points, and some small; and some are curved, and some straight. And of these there are three-angled ones. And of these there are language arrows. And of these there are tethers.
Indeed the ones that enter the limbs fall according to two modes. Or if they enter the principal limbs, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and those like them. When therefore it falls into one of these limbs, and you see signs of death, which I will describe to you afterwards, then you should extract the arrow from it, for death follows it in many dispositions. And when you do not see these bad signs, and the arrow does not appear to be embedded deeply in the limb, then you should extract it, and take care of the wound.
For when the arrow falls into the brain and penetrates the skull and exits through a sinus that is above the brain, it causes a violent fever, and opacities in the eyes, swelling, dizziness, redness of the eyes, blushing, spasms, confusion of thought, and loss of memory, and cholera. And perhaps it will exit blood from the nose, or ears. And perhaps speech is severed, and language is destroyed. And it will exit from the wound a white moisture similar to spittle. And it will exit from it a liquid-like meat.
If you see these signs, then refrain from treatment, and extract the arrow if it has not already exited.
The signs of an arrow that falls into the heart are: it is close to the left nipple; and feels as if it were fixed in a hard object, not in a soft one. And the arrows move in a way similar to the pulsation; and black blood runs from the ulnar bone. And then follows the cold of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting. So know that death has come near.
And when the arrow hits the lung, there is a spray of dark blood from the ulnar bone; and the veins that run along the neck, turn pale, and change color in the nurse's hands. And he breathes out great breath, deep; and seeks the coolness of air.
That if the arrow falls into a hole, which is in the chest: then it is near the back for a few inches; and it causes great pain with a sharp ache, with a sigh: and all the arms of the shoulders move.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then it is likely that something undigested has come out of the ulnar bone from food.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach and remains there: then something like dung comes out of the ulnar bone, or a knuckle, or an intestine that has already been broken. So there is no hope for his cure, nor for the extraction of the arrow.
That if the arrow falls into the bladder and comes out as urine: and it proceeds from it something that goes outside, and the pain is stronger on the sick person: then know that it is fatal.
The rest of the limbs, such as the face, and the neck, and the tongue, and the spatula, and the aid, and the spine, and the fork, and the hip, and the leg, and those similar to the limbs are healed according to many dispositions: but if the arrow does not hit an artery or a nerve; and there is no deadly arrow.
And I tell you what I saw of these arrows, that you may understand with regard to your own cure.
That is, for a certain man, an arrow fell into his eye, into the lacrimal eye, at the root of his nose. Therefore I removed it from him on one side, below the fat of his ear; and he was healed, and there was no injury to his eye.
And I took out another arrow from the Jew, which had fallen to him in the fat of his eye below his lower eyelid; and the arrow was already hidden, and I did not succeed in taking it out except for its end, which touched his skin, having been wedged in his wood: and this was a great arrow of Turkish arrows, with a soft iron, in which the ears are not.
So the Jew was healed, and there was no harm to his eye.
And I took out another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And this was an Arabic arrow, and it had ears. So I followed up on top of him between two teeth: and it was already submerged in his throat. I was delicate when I took it out, until I took it out: and the Christian was healed.
And I took out another arrow from a man which had fallen to him in his belly; and we thought that he would die from it.
Then after forty days I passed on behind him, and there was nothing changed in his appearance from his habits, so I followed up the arrow; and I carefully considered it over, and took it out: and he was healed, and there was no harm to him.
And I saw a man to whom the arrow had fallen on his back, and a piece of wood had been set over it. Then after seven years the arrow came out at the root of his thigh.
And I saw a woman to whom an arrow had fallen into her belly, and a piece of wood had been set over it, and the arrow remained within her; and she did not change from her habits in anything, and the woman did not find any harm in him from his natural operations.
And I saw another man to whom an arrow had fallen on his face; and a piece of wood had been set over it, and it remained, not finding much pain in him: and there were many similar things like this.
I extracted a arrow from the king's face, which had fallen on his middle nose, and had already inclined towards the right side by about as much: and the whole arrow was hidden.
So I was called to cure him after the arrow fell for three days. Then I found a sturdy arrow shaft.
So I examined it with a careful touch, and did not feel it out: and he himself felt the prick and pain on his right side. I hoped that was a prick from the end of the arrow.
I plastered over the place with a plaster that attracts and draws on its strength, and assured myself, that if the place were to heal up, I would show you a sign of the arrow, so that I might cut off what followed it: and no such accident happened, meaning that the arrow had gone straight.
I applied the plaster over it for many days: and no accident happened, and the bone was consolidated in between: and he remained weak because of having had the arrow extracted from him for a space of days, until one day he felt the arrow within his nose. And he told me about that.
And he told me about that. So I put on the bone an acute medicine, which I kept for many days, until it was open: and when I examined him, I felt a slight bump of the arrow that clung to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound with that acute medicine, until we could see clearly the end of the arrow: and there passed away from me with that space of time about four months.
Then after the bone had grown bigger, and might possibly have been inserted into it with a fork, and moved it back and forth: then I did not cease to be careful, and devised ways for it, until one day I came upon it with a pair of forks made shorter, according to which their form became at the end of the chapter, so that I extracted it. Then I healed the fracture.
And the doctors say that if the nasal cartilage is not restored, the nose will be disfigured. I then restored it, and the fracture was consolidated, and the broken part was healed with complete health: nor did it cause him any harm at all.
And I will tell you the quality of a certain type of incision: so that you may place that rule and meaning over it, which I have not said.
For the parts and distinctions of this art are not contained in the telling: nor does that book contain them, and the crafty workman compares little with much: and when he describes what is present, he makes mention of what is absent: and he discovers a new operation, and a new instrument, on account of the descent of foreign things of this art when they occur.
I say then, that the arrows do not come out of the member from which they are fixed, except according to two ways. Or by being drawn out of a place into which it entered. Or by being drawn on the contrary side of another part.
And that which goes out from where it entered, or is an arrow going into flesh: and being drawn, it goes out.
That if it does not yield to the exit at the time when it falls: then you must leave it for a few days until the flesh which is around it rots: and the attraction of it will be allayed, and its exit will be eased.
And likewise if it is broken into a bone, and does not yield to you at the exit, then leave it for several days: and again and again with attraction and motion every day until it goes out.
That if it does not yield to you at the exit after several days, then you must cut around the arrow in the bone with a sharp knife, until the arrows become larger. Then attract it, and take it out.
But if the arrow is now buried deep in the body, and hidden and concealed from sensation: then feel for it with your hand. But if you feel it, then attract it with some instruments suitable for attracting it.
But if you cannot bring it to the surface because of the constriction of the wound, and because of the length of the arrow deep inside: and there is no bone, nerve, or vessel there: then look for it until the limb becomes bigger, and it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
That if they are ears which it holds fast, then free the flesh that is fixed to them on all sides, with all the skill you can muster, and the skill of others also: but if it cannot be brought to bear on the ears, or their distortion, until he is healed.
And you, when you perform the extraction of the arrow, wherever it is, then use your twisted hands with forceps to all parts, until you make it bleed: and the smallest detail, so that the arrow does not break, and it becomes difficult for you to attract it, and to extract it.
That if you cannot bring it out all at once, then leave it for a few days, until what is around it begins to rot. Then repeat the extraction: for surely bandages ease pain: but if you feel opposition from the flow of blood, then administer what I have said in my book on healing: and guard your skill from cutting into bone, or a nerve, or a vessel.

Regarding phlebotomy (bloodletting) from veins.
The veins which are phlebotomized, of which custom runs in the body, are thirty.
Of these in the head are sixteen: two pulsating veins, which run behind the ears, called the two herbs; and two arteries, which appear in two tempi; and two veins, which run in two nostrils of two eyes, called the two ASPECTIVE; and a vein prepared in the middle of the forehead; and a vein at the end of the nose. And two guide-veins, which are in the neck. And two veins, which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two wounds that are in the lower lip. These are wounds which are called Algecirich. And these two wounds that are beneath the tongue.
But the veins which are phlebotomized in the arms, are five wounds in the whole arm.
One of them is Cephalica, and it is from the lateral forest; and it is called by the common name a wound of the head. And Fusca. And the middle wound, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilical and a branch of the Cephalical: and it is called by the common name a wound of the body. And the Basilical, which is placed on the lateral domestic: and it is also called the Titillicaris. And this one is called a wound of the leg. And theFunis brachii, and it is placed on the back; and this one is that which appears above the little finger by a visible indentation.
And the Salutatella: and this one is between the ring finger and the little finger; and these two are the branches.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three wounds. One of them is that which is beneath the little finger's knuckle, from the lateral forest. And the second one is the Saphenous vein; and its place is at the heel bone on the lateral domestic side. And the third one, which is called the Nerve; and its place is at the heel bone from the lateral forest.
And in the other leg there are three similar wounds.

On the Benefit of Phlebotomy and Care to be Taken with It. The Quality or Advantage of Phlebotomy, and the Consequences of It; by which You Should Take Thought for Correction.
Then first of all you should know that phlebotomy is not performed except for the preservation of health, and its perseverance, and the care to avoid any illness.
And so that phlebotomy may be performed on one of the three which are in a bed, namely the Cephalica, and the Fusca, and the Basilica. And so that phlebotomy may be performed at the beginning of spring on a fast, and during the summer, and autumn after a meal, when there are signs of fullness.
And so that phlebotomy may be performed on the first or third day after having passed three hours since the last day.
But phlebotomy which is performed in illness has not determined time; but when it occasioned convenience and necessity to it, at night, or by day, and at all times, and in all places.
And it is not necessary that boys should be phlebotomized until they are fourteen years old. Nor should a woman who is pregnant be phlebotomized until the fourth month up to the seventh. Nor should elderly people who have passed sixty years be phlebotomized.
When then whoever desires to be phlebotomized for any reason should wash his bowels well before phlebotomy with a laxative, if there is much feces in them: so that the fresh blood does not attract the eyes of the phlebotomist from the intestines when it flows, which injures the chief parts.
Do not let the patient suffer from fainting, that is, nausea; nor do they have a cramp, nor does their sweat come from drunkenness until they get rid of it themselves. Nor is the patient ill with hydrops.
And let the phlebotomy be repeated after a colic passion, and vomiting, and flow of blood, and increase of sexual intercourse, and work, and exercise, and vigils, and fasting, and any that dissolves virtue out of the bodily or animal realm.
Then consider with subtlety the humors before that, if the blood is thick with food and drink and medicines: if it can be made possible for him.
Then let him bathe in cold water, if he does not forbid anything: or let him exercise himself in some kind of exercise, that the blood may be refined.
And let his phlebotomy be placed in his chest, about the third day as we have said: and be careful so that his chest is emptied of all animal bad accidents on that day: such as anger, fear: and all body accidents, such as work, punishment, excesses, and the like.
And let him make his own presence of such things with which custom of men runs according to the use: from aromatizing, and fragrance, and revelry, and similar things through quantity of possibility.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a raised chair, that it may be higher than the one where phlebotomy is done: then he shall take his arm out and rub it with his own hand twice, or three times. Then let him tighten the ligature with a strap, and turn it two times.
And let the constriction be of moderate tension, for a constriction that is uneven, or by excess in the constriction, then it forbids the course of blood: or in softening, then also it forbids the course of blood.
Then after the constriction, let the phlebotomist rub his hands together, face to face, until they are warmed, and present a sensation to the sense.
Then the phlebotomist dried off the bleeding with a little oil, according to custom.
Then he put his index finger of his left hand on the vein where he wanted to take the bloodletting, a little: so that the blood doesn't run away, and so that the pulsation isn't interrupted. Since it is from the veins that bleeding takes place like a running string during bloodletting, and from those that are full of blood: and when you put the phlebotome on it, it is pressed under the phlebotome, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and it does not open the vein: and if it does open it, it is tight. Therefore it is necessary for the phlebotomist to tighten it, and be diligent in these things.
Then put down the phlebotome. If then one vein opens out of many, then it is good. And if not, then go back to the other vein a little below that one, or above it with a rush: if not, choose a place.
Which if it is put out of joint, or causes pain to the patient, then let go of it for a day, or two days: and do not tighten the ligament. For maybe it draws the blood because of the heat. And don't put it in the bath.
Then go back to the bloodletting, if he loves it.
Which if the opening is small, or the blood runs gently: and be careful not to lose a quantity of blood which you wish to take from him: then go back with the phlebotome into that same hole, and add a little to the procedure.

For the blood is thinned and resolved when it is thick.
