And the ventilations that are administered with scarring and extraction of blood are four-십여 places in the body. Of which one is Ventilation of the Crown, and it is lastly of the head: and Alkhel, and is in the middle of the neck: and ventilations of Al-Hadhdahim, and they are two layers of the neck on both sides simultaneously: and ventilations of the mind, and is under the mandible in the lower mouth: and ventilations of two spatulas: and ventilations of Al-La'il at the end of the tail: and ventilations of two fires, and they are in the middle of the two arms: and ventilations of two chaps: and ventilations of two calves.
And indeed does not extract blood from you from small sparse veins in the flesh, and because of it does not fall virtue from them, such as is the case with phlebotomy. It is not appropriate that the application of tar be done with fire or without fire in any illness that occurs from fullness until the whole body is emptied.
If necessity compels to the application of tar in illness or by custom, we shall administer it at the beginning, end, and middle of the month, and at any time.
That is because a person becomes bald when blood multiplies within him until it needs to be extracted by tar application, and finds himself bald, and does not remain except with shaving.
And among them is he who finds fullness and redness on his face, his head, and his neck. And among them is he who finds itching on his face, his forehead, darkness, and dullness in his eyes.
And among them is he who scratches the place where he applied tar. And among them is he whose laughter multiplies. And among them is he who finds the taste of blood in his mouth, or who spits it out with his tongue, and does not remain except by drinking blood. And among them is he whose sleep multiplies. And among them is he who sees in his sleep blood and redness, and those who have been slain, and plagues, and similar things.
When we see any of them, and especially if it is in the middle of the third month: We order tar application to be done after two or three hours have passed from the day.
But the application of tar on the back is this: because it brings baldness to the head, and it drips down to the eyes. But it is appropriate that it be done after the whole body has been emptied.
And this tar is a place of phlebotomy on the head.
And it is not appropriate that he who has a cold complexions should use it, or in whom there is a croupy cough. For it causes great harm.
Since they are not old enough to use these, nor do they have any diseases of cold in their heads.
But those who become too accustomed to it, lose their memory.
And for this reason, you should tell Ventosator to put his hand down a little when he is blowing on it, out of fear of losing his memory.
Ventosation or Al-Khel is the place of the phlebotomy of Fusca, and the phlebotomy of Basilica, and for this reason it confers asa-mus, and constriction of anhelius, and fissures of the instrument of anhelius, and coughs, and fulfillment.
And it should be that Ventosator is blown a little by Al-Khel, because if it is put down, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Ventosation on the other hand, confers pain on accidental injuries in the head, and on the eyes, and on headaches, and on piercing, and on pain in the roots of the teeth; and it is the place of the phlebotomy of Basilica.
And you should tell Ventosator not to touch the skin with his scarification instrument so that he does not hit an artery and cause a flow of blood.
But Ventosation under the chin, confers alcohol in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar diseases that are in the mouth: and it is the place of a different kind of phlebotomy, which is in the two lips.
But Ventosation with the spatula, confers to Al-Khajah, which is done by a replexion and heat.
Ventosation of the inner parts confers a favor to phlebotomy of the three veins, Basilica, Fusca, and Cephalica: because she herself attracts blood from all those small veins that are in the flesh: and these small veins attract those other larger veins until they reach the attraction of the three.
It is necessary that you do not let the scalpel go too far into the scarification: for the place is stripped of flesh, and under it are nerves and arteries.
But the ventosa which is applied to the anus, it brings hemorrhoids and ulcers of the lower parts.
And you should order the scalpel to be big, and that it is made of iron: because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps the fine scalpel are broken: and let the scarification be done with a big scarification.
But the ventosa of the legs diminishes the visible reduction: because they attract blood from all over the body, and bring old pains in the veins, and membranes, and vesicles: they induce menstruation, and bring fever, and constriction: and stand in the place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and alhorcobeni.
But they weaken the body very much, and make many people faint.
And the alhorcobeni of the ventosa is a near relationship to the iuuamento of the two legs of the ventosa.
The quality of the position of the ventosa is that you put the ventosa in the first place, and suck with moderate suction: and do not prolong the position of the ventosa, but put them quickly, and move them quickly, so that they go to the place of exudation equally: and do not stop repeating this and continuing, until you see the place already red and swollen, and the blood appears: then therefore scarify, and repeat the suction slowly. Then consider in the disposition of the bodies.
For he who is of tender flesh, and few pores, it should be scarified once only, not more: lest the place be ulcerated.
And order the scalpel to make the scarification bigger, and deeper a little: and moderate the suction in ease, and speed, and smooth motion.
That if there is grossness in the blood, then it should be that you scarify him twice. Indeed, the first incision opens the way for the subtlety of blood, and its watering; and, indeed, the second is for the exquisite examination of the extraction of gross blood.
If the blood is indeed putrid, then let the third incision be made: so that the result may be the last.
And to the end, when we wish to extract blood little by little, or a little at a time, one incision is sufficient for us. That if we wish to extract blood many times, then let us make multiple incisions.
And if we measure, that the blood is thick: then it is necessary so that a deep incision should be made: and the equal term in the incision, is the depth of the skin.
What is that which needs to be administered from oils at the placement of vents, and what does it cause?
IN that which has the skin thick, hard, dark and whose pores are tight, one should anoint the places of the Vents with open, soothing, حل  oils: if it is in the time of summer, such as is a strong oil, or a mild oil, or an almond oil, or a seed oil of cucumber.
And if it is in the time of winter, then olive oil, or violet oil, or chamomile oil, or sage oil: and these are similar.
If the superfluity is thick, cold: then let there be bitter oil, or olive oil, or alburnum oil, or annerbitz oil: and these are similar.
If the wind is of large pores, tender flesh: then one should prohibit from oil, and it should be washed after ventilation with rosy water, or cold water, or sun water, or cucumber water, or portulaca water: and these are similar.
But that which has blood of much moisture, washes the vents with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumach: and these are similar.
Whoseever are the fluid excesses, wash them away with old wine, or with water of samshuk, or with a decoction of aniseed, or of chamomile; and for these alike.
And it is necessary that the cooling should be done in a bath or in a foot-bath; and it is necessary also that an administrate after leaving the bath for one hour, or two hours.
And it is not necessary that anyone should sleep after cooling.
What is it, then, for which the treatment of cooling and phlebotomy is administered before cooling?
And after this, it is necessary that you consider first of all: if the person is bilious and his blood is sharp and inflamed, then he should take cooling things, such as granatum muzum; also vinegar and lettuce, with secani-bin and juleb; and similar things.
And put before him the foods that dry up his flesh, and meat of a kind to make him thirsty, and prepared foods with grapes: and similar things.
And if his disposition is cold, he should take in a drink of syrup of honey, or of mulberry, or of secani-bin made from seeds; and he should take medium wine that is sharp, which is between sweet and sour; and let it be commanded that he eat sparingly. And put before him the foods that dry up his flesh, and tarragon; and pigeons, that is, many birds: and let him eat pigeon-meat often baked.
And it is necessary that the drink of the day after cooling and phlebotomy be stronger than food; and it is necessary that a medicine be given in a drink to some men containing tyrrhene or alfaroch, or a remedy made from musk, that is musca-ta, or alselith before cooling: and before phlebotomy, or after it, so as to comfort the main limbs, and subtilize the blood. They should not take it out of a hot pot.
On Ventosis without scarring. But the venesections that are without scarring, are venesections that are put on the skin, and the liver, and the spleen, and on the belly, and places of the ribs, and the anus: for these limbs do not endure the scarification on themselves.
And it is not sought after them, except that the blood flow from one member to another: as we put the venesection on the breasts in a flu of blood from the nose. And we use them, that we solve a cold venesection on one member to another: as we put the venesection on the belly, and the umbilical cord. For it itself tightens the member, and warms it, and moves away the pain, because it has solved the venesection.
Also is put on the kidneys, when it happens to them a deposit, or a stone. For with his virtue of attraction he might perhaps open a deposit, or eradicate a stone from its place.
And also does when it is put on the liver, and the spleen next to a venesection that should heal in them both: for he moves away the pain from them.
And these Venesections are administered with a sharp knife with only a slight cut. And when they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in the case of a pleural illness: and that is so, so that the venesection is large, and there is only warm water, or water in which herbs have been cooked.
Form of administration of the Venesections with fire.
Let the width of his opening be two fingers wide, according to which we made it: and let the quantity of it in the depth be half a palm: and let it be in the back of its side which is near the middle of it, small hole, second to the quantity that it is cooked inside itself: so it is made from brass, or yellow copper, large margin, soft, equal, third: so that it does not hurt the member at its position on itself: and let it be in the middle of it a wire crossing from brass, or iron, where a candle is put with fire.
And this has also been very useful, greater than we have described, and less by a little, according to the disease and age of him who uses it.
For young people, the winds are useful, and others, which weaken, greater than the winds that men use, and for those who have large bodies.
The quality of this wind-making when set on fire on a limb is such, that you kindle the linden wood with fire from off-center linen, or place a small candle made of wax: and put it on the middle of the arm across-way that lies in the middle of the wind-making. so that the fire all around may be lower than the wind-making, so that the body is not burned up.
Then let it be placed on the limb: and let the finger be placed over the opening that I said, as long as we hold the wind-making: while we are holding it, let us take off the finger, and steam will come out over that opening, and the wind-making will immediately cease.
Then set fire to the linden wood in the manner described, and do this again if you can bear it.
But the wind-making that is administered in pleural pain with water has no across-way limb or opening; and it is not administered, unless it fills with water, and is placed on the limb only.
This is its form.
And this wind-making is the better when it is larger, so that it takes in a lot of water, for that is good.
Regarding the suspension of blood-sugar.
Blood-sugar is not administered in many, unless the limbs are unable to take wind-making, because of their smallness, such as the lips, and gums, and things like them. Or in a naked limb, like the finger, and the nose, and things like them.
And the quality of using these is such: that you may come from blood-sugar which are in sweet waters, clean from corruption.
Then they are to be discharged and given sweet water to drink at night until they become hungry, and there is nothing left in their bellies. Then the body is to be evacuated first with bloodletting, and then with vomiting. Then the nurse washes the member with water, until it turns red. Then they place them on top of him.
When therefore they are filled, and fall down, and it is possible to suck the place with a poultice, then it is more final in healing: and if not, then the place is to be washed with vinegar. Then with a lot of water, and it is to be rubbed, and pressed out.
If the course of blood is prolonged after the fall of blood from the wound, and it is that residue: then put a linen cloth in cold water, and place him upside down, until the residue falls off.
If the blood multiplies, then dust is to be sprinkled on him of oak bark, or of hickory, and similar things from a styptic: until the blood falls off.
Or that they are placed on top of the place half with ground oak bark: and they are to be left until the oak bark adheres to the place, for the blood is shed.
And it is necessary if the necessity requires a change of the wound, when it is possible, to change them.
If however they are prohibited from suspension, then the place is to be cleaned with recent blood. Or bind them now on the places, until something exits from the blood. Then they are to be placed, for when they feel some of the blood, they adhere immediately.
When therefore you wish them to fall down, sprinkle on them something from freshly squeezed aloe, or salt, or ash: and they fall down immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary in the art of Medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures, and dislocations of accidents in bones.
Know, O Sons, for this chapter is stolid to the doctors and people, and they have never examined the ancient book, nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason, for that this way of treatment is hidden from the sciences in our land. And I was not satisfied with it at all, and I have not been fully convinced by it unless because of the length of reading the ancient books, and study on their understanding, until I drew out the science of that from them.
Then, after I had come to the conclusion and study of a long life.
And then, I have described to you from that whole what contains my science: and beyond that which passed through my experiment, after I shortened it for you, and freed it from the occupation of elongation, and shortened it end by abbreviation, and showed it end by declination: and I formed in it many forms from forms of instruments which are administered to it, with the addition of a declination, as I did in two preceding Chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary that we say in the prolog of this chapter, the summary of the speech, and chapters, to which the understanding of the craftsman on the station of their health: namely, so that you, and who are eager, know, or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, then, that when it happens to anyone a fracture, or dislocation, or twist, or fall: then you should prepare first to phlebotomize him, and to solve it itself, or both at the same time: if anything prohibits this, such as weakness of virtue: or he is one whose accidebit something from the boy, or the old man's decrepitude: or is the time of a high fever, or cold very much.
Then be satisfied with his food, cold drinks, and meat of birds and beasts; and prohibit wine, thick flesh, and all food which fills the veins with blood. Until he is completely cured from his fever and does not vomit matter into the limbs: then let the sick one return to his routine care first with which his custom runs.
When the broken jaw begins to be healed, it is necessary that the sick eat food which is very nourishing and thick, in which there is a lot of juice: such as bread made from wheat cooked with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and livers of animals, and eggs, and recent fish: and strong wine, and similar things. For with this regimen the clotting of the fracture is speeded up, and better.
And know that bones which are broken, when they are in years of senility and decrepitude, cannot continue to heal and harden after their nature always: but they heal and become firm in the end of leniity, like the bones of young boys of few years.
However, nature fixes broken bone from all parts with some kind of glue, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and tightens to itself, until it joins together, and binds together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until it does not prevent its functions: and for this reason it is necessary that you put nourishing food into the sick person's stomach, in which there are fats, and juice, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the species of fractures are different, according to the difference of the limbs: for a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the head-bone: and a fracture of the breast-bone is different from a fracture of the back-bone: and likewise all other limbs differ from each other: and I come with a reminder of all species of fractures mentioned in their separate chapters from others. They also specified the species of bone fractures and even in themselves.
For the fracture is curved, except that which happens in it, the spines. And when the fracture is according to the length of the bone: and are fractures of his leg, that is to say, thorns, and additions proceeding, such as are in the reed, when it is caught with a knife for writing. And not proceeding: and is the fracture with the ulna and dislocation in the head: and is the fracture small crack.
And all kinds of art are there, or their own instruments for repairing it, according to the memory of it, distinct in its places.
And from that which is known the fracture of the bone, is its bend, and its eminence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
And when there is no bend in place, and the sound does not appear to you at your touch, when you press the bone with agitation, and it finds no great pain: then is not there a fracture, but possible that it be a bend, or small crack: so there is no need for you to move it by hand and compression at all, except put over it from among the physicians, of whose memory has come, after time it that comes to place: and then tighten it with a thin knot.
And know that the place when it bends, and is divided into two parts, except that which happens in it, thorns: but all part is removed from its companion: then you need to begin at your hours to straighten it, making equal before it comes the apoplexy of heat.
But if it happens to him the heat of apoplexy, then you abandon it for a few days, until he rests the apoplexy. Then make equal the bone according to any way you can from its slowness and art.
And know that the restoration of it, and its equality, is easier than the bones, in which happen thorns.
Then tighten it up, according to how you want to remember it.
If, however, the fractures are in the bones of the hand or foot, then it is necessary to extend this broken member from two sides, whether with your hand or with two strings, or with one string and one hand, or with two hands.
And your position should be such that you place the member on an equal location, according to its natural shape, until the parts of the broken member are extended: then strive, so that you can return these additions with all ingenuity, and in any way you can in their places: and carefully, according to your strength, so that pain does not come to the weak through your operation, or injury: and strive, according to your strength, that you apply one or two bones with his partner, according to better shapes.
And it is necessary at that hour for you to try and touch it with your hand. If, then, you see something far away, straighten it out, and make it equal in length to the amount of your strength.
And strive for a smooth extension, and a strong compression, as many do with the stupids. For many times, such a thing has happened to them through their own operation, hot or contracted in the member: as we have already seen this from their operations many times.
Then, after you have made it equal, and the operation is done, and tighten the member, and rest, and peace: and warn the weak about it. And let him try to calm down, so that he does not move himself at the time of his illness, and his sleep during his crisis, and his agitation during his digestion, and all his movements in his last strength. And if he tries to calm down, so that the position of the member is not a position that causes pain. That is, because when he feels pain in the position of the member's injury: then it is necessary that you change it to a different position from which he does not feel pain.
And care with him, that his position may be equal and straight; lest there be curvature of the member when he is restored.
And the quality of a broken bone's stricture is, according to which narrows.
You should know that broken limbs differ in their healing, and in their size and shape.
Which of them are small, such as the arm, and finger, and foot, and the like, then it is necessary that there be a softening of the soft cloth, thin. Which of them are thick, such as the hip and leg, then it is necessary that there be a broad softening, hard.
Since a broad ligament connects the large member, and tightens it with equal stricture, so that it does not enter it spot.
Then begin after you have drained it to place the liniment suitable for it, in a soft spot on the site of the fracture. Then begin to soften the ligament over the site of the fracture four times, or as much as the limb deserves; and grip your hand lightly with the ligament.
Then move it with him to the upper side of the site of the fracture, and grip less than what your own stricture was, which you gripped the broken place. Then lengthen yourself with the softening slightly from the site of the fracture, and soften the stricture gradually, until you take hold of a large object in place of the healthy member.
Then take another, and soften it again over the broken site with the liniment. Then move with it to the lower side of the fracture. And let your operation work on the softening and hardening, according to which we said in the first upper softening.
Then place between the softings a soft cloth, or cloth, with which to equalize the curvature of the fracture, if there is any curvature in it; and if not, do not place anything in it.
Then take off the bandage and examine the wound again. Then immediately remove the bandages that were applied to the wounds, if there is no inflammation or swelling, and check for any signs of infection.
If there is inflammation or a boil: then place something that will help the boil dry up and remove the inflammation; and let it go for a few days. Then tighten the bandage on the wound.
And let the bandages be made of pieces of soft leather, shaped wisely; or of pieces of wood from which are made mesh-like screens, made of pine wood or of splinters, that is to say, of palm branches, or of reeds: and let the size of each bandage be according to this form and figure.
However, it is necessary that the bandage which is placed on the wound should be a little broader and longer than the others; and its length should be determined by the size of the member and its proportion.
Then tighten the bandages with another one, following the first one's shape. Then tie it with loose knots at the top, as we have said before concerning the tightness; and let the tightness be greater than the broken part's location: and to the extent that the break extends, make the tightness less.
And let the middle threads in thickness and fine be made of soft leather.
For if the threads are too thick, as happens when they are made from the tendon-like cord of a sheep's belly after it has been stretched, this is a great mistake; for the tightness falls outside its limit.
And very fine threads do not suit either; for you will not achieve what you want by tying with such thin threads.
And it is not right that there should be less than the distance between a finger and its tip between one bandage and another.
For if a sick person is bound up by the ends of the bandages after the tightness at the healthy places: then place something soft under the bandages, or red wool, until there is no pain from it.
When however there is a fracture with the ulna, and there occurs a rupture of the skin, I will come to it with its consideration in the second chapter on its uniqueness.
And know that not every broken limb needs to be held with splints from the first day. That is, because the limb when it is big, does not need to be put on top of splints, unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your security from the risk of oozing pus.
And this is the method of splints which the ancients used, which are placed on top of the fracture and dislocation.
The common shortened method of the splint, restoring the fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children; because it is not from that one which hurts heat, nor cold.
And it is so that you take from the meal to be ground, and there is flour that hangs on the walls of the grinder at the movement of the mill; and you put it as it is, except that you comb it with white mortar: and you put its coating not thick, nor thin. Then use it.
The other method of the splint, restoring the fracture, and dislocation, and torsion. Take alum, and olive-tree, and acacia, and goat's hair, and mug; and juice of all 10 myrrh, and aloe of both sexes 5 and alethil 20 let them be mixed with water of alethil, or with white mortar, if the complexion of the wound is warmed. Then use this splint. For this splint is good composition, restores bones quickly, and suits many people.
The other method of the splint, which also suits fractures and torsion. Take mug, and alum, and alethia white, all 10 myrrh, and aloe of both sexes 5 and acacia 6 and bolus of Armenia 20 let them be mixed with a lot of water, or with white mortar, and let them be applied.
The mode of preparing poultices for bandages, and separate bones from their places; it will soothe their pain, injury, bone-cutting, and fracture. Take wet wool and dip it in vinegar and oil-infused wine; and place it on the spot.
And in this (type of poultice) there is no virtue of restoring health, but it is good for soothing boil-like swelling: and it drives away its own pains.
The mode of poulticing, which restores a broken bone. Take leaves from the oak tree and leaves from the plantain tree; and dry them thoroughly, and make a poultice out of both.
The mode of a shortened poultice, applied at the restoration of a bone fracture; and when you wish to resolve a boil-like swelling. Recollect root of the aloes plant, and camomile, and flowers of violets, and herb flour; all in equal parts: dry them thoroughly, and make a paste with wine, if it is not a member that needs to be warmed.
If it (the member) does need to be warmed, then make the paste with water and coriander seed, or with water alone, and use it.
The mode of a stronger poultice for resolving a boil-like swelling during the restoration of a member, rather than this one first, when it occurs. Recollect root of the aloes plant, and flax seed, and poplar bud, and mallows, and water-leaf plant, and flower of violet, and camomile; all in equal parts, dry them thoroughly, and make a paste with oak-water, or with sweet water, or with wine. All this depends on the heat of the member, and the relief from heat.
Hippocrates did not say, in his book, that a poultice should be placed on a broken member for its restoration, except one made from cerates and oil; nothing else: he related, that it should be of an intermediate nature between gruel and lightness.
Galen, however, considered it essential to apply these remedies on a broken limb while restoring its function, where there is drying up of heat, such as myrrh, and aloes and olive resin, and similar substances from those which we have mentioned.
The mode of use of the plaster which is useful for wounds and pain: Take myrrh, and chickpeas, and hair of men's heads, or feathers of birds, and resin, and equal parts of salt: crush them, and pound them, and form them into a plaster.
The quantity however, according to what is necessary, so that the bandage remains on, then dissolves, is such, as you consider then, if no pain does not happen, and itching, and the broken bone does not move from its place: then do not dissolve it over several days. If however it happens to him in the place severe itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling: then proceed, and dissolve at once, and remove the plaster from him: and take a cloth of linen, or sea sponge of linen, wet with water: and dip it into the water, and wash with it the place, until the itching is calm, and the pain subsides.
If however it happens to him in the place severe itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling: then proceed, and dissolve at once, and remove the plaster from him: and take a cloth of linen, or sea sponge of linen, wet with water: and dip it into the water, and wash with it the place, until the itching is calm, and the pain subsides. Then let go of the limb for a rest of one hour. Then put over it a cloth of linen dipped in vinegar and oil, or in oil infused with wine: and bind it on for the night, so that he does not come in contact with any hot thing, and the swelling of the limb subsides, and the pain of his member is calmed.
Then repeatedly apply to him a slight tightening, and a small plaster: and do not tighten him with your own hand first, and fine things, until he is healed.
That if you see that the swelling, and redness, and pain, and swelling, and all accidents are completely gone away, and you do not feel either the plaster or the tightening, then again apply to him as you did before equally:
If any of these things do not happen to a member, then do not remove it, unless after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when the member is removed after forty days as it appears to you from the removal of the member, as we said, until the fracture is united and the clotting of the flesh over it: add also then more than your first stricture in all its parts: and add also in the increase of food sickness, according as preceded the recollection of it.
But if you see the place of the fracture already drying up and growing less than is necessary: then know that food should be forbidden to reach it. So wrap up the member with warm water at every joint, and let it rest for three days, and allow a little bit of pain.
By this operation runs towards the member food, and heals quickly.
But that which the stupid surgeons do from the fracture of a member, when they do not restore it first, according as it should be restored, and restore it according to the curvature, is an error from their operation, and a great fear: and if it had been right, the ancients in their books would have said so, and would have operated with it.
And I did not see anywhere their trace in that at all, and it is better that it should not be made.
On making an accident in the head.
There are various kinds of fractures and many forms of it: and its causes are separate.
For from the fracture is caused by a blow, and the blow carries away the whole bone as far as it reaches to the socket, which is under the bone, just as the ax does in the wood: and therefore this kind of fracture is called the axial fracture.
Or a blade cuts some bone, and cuts its face, and does not pierce the incision as far as its last part: and is called this kind of fracture the Kayan, that is, an absolute slant.
And it is a break of two fractures, or great, or small.
And from the fracture there is a rupture, or rubbing, and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also penetrating, which is beginning to approach the skin, which is under the bone: or it is on the face of the bone: and it is a break of these two fractures as well, either small, or great.
And from the fracture is that which is hidden in the bone in subtlety of a hair: and it is a small crack, and for this reason this kind of fracture is called a capillary fracture.
And from these is a fracture which is made by a fall, or by the impact of a stone, and similar things, which makes it so that a layer of bone is crushed and crushed on the inside: as happens in the case of pot-ash, when it falls upon it and it is crushed on the inside.
And indeed this often happens to that in the heads of moist bones, as are the heads of children.
And all these kinds of fracture separate and I remember them all together in their place.
And we know all these kinds of fractures by detection over them, and by questioning them with probes, and by cutting away the corrupted flesh that is on them.
Indeed this kind of Capillary fracture is recognized when it appears on the bone, and is scrubbed, and is coated on the top with caustics: for a black spot appears.
In the treatment of a fracture, first consider these nursing matters: if you see from these matters that which means a clear meaning on worry, such as cholera breath, and spreading, and loss of intellect, and deafness, and sudden speaking, and high fever, and red eyes, and similar things to these accidents in many dispositions, it is certainly not cured: and if you see that which does not terrify you, and you hope for healing: then begin its treatment.
If he comes to you wounded in the beginning when he is wounded: and that it is on the half-days, then you should study in the removal of bone before the fourteenth day according to all the arrangement.
And if it is on the days of summer, then you should remove the bone before the seventh day before it corrupts what is under the bone from a flap, and those accidents which we have described.
That if the fracture of the bone has already reached the flap touching the brain, and it is with small ones, and by attraction: then you should break off the broken part, crushed, according to what I tell you.
And it is, so that you shave the head of the wounded person: and you should detect above the bone according to any possible way for you: and according to the figure of the wound, and what is light is above the weak.
That if it happens to you in the detection of the bone, the flow of blood or warm pulp: then you should oppose it with that which is necessary. And it is, so that you fill the place with rags soaked in wine, and olive oil: and make the bone rest until the pus stops, and you are safe from the flow of blood. Then begin in the cure of the bone, and in its removal.
And that should be done according to one of two ways.
The way is one, so that you break the bone with a sharp chisel: and this is the form of it.
And there is another way, so that you administer an Incisory other after an Incisory wider by a little bit: and this is the form of it.
Then also administer an Incisory other wider than the second.
And the second way is, so that it is with you a number of Incisors different: some are wider to some, and some of them are shorter to some: and let there be at the end of their acuity their extremities, and let they be made of iron, or oil: and let you use convenience in striking over the Incisor, so that it does not wound the head, and hurts itself.
If the jaw is strong and firm: then you should perform incisions around it before administering incisions with blades that do not cut deep: and they are not called so unless they do not pass beyond the bone to that which is behind it, because the temporal bone is round at its extremities, similar in shape to the tip of the jaw, and a small circle prevents it from sinking and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting that you take from these temporals a large number, of which each corresponds to the thickness of the bone, until you have all the cranial Temporals: the extremities of whose sharp ends are in length and brevity according to the thickness of its bone.
And this is the shape of the three types of temporal bones, or craniums.
The quality of the cut around the fractured bone is: when you place the temporal bone on the bone and rotate it with your fingers, until you know that the bone has already penetrated. Then change the temporal bone to another place, and put what is between all the pores of its thickness or around it.
Then cut with the blades between all the pores from the bone, and make it thinner than you can from its edge of lightness, as we have said, until you have cut through the bone, or with your hand, or with any other tool that you have prepared for it: such as scalpel and fine forceps.
And you should be careful not to touch the temporal bone or any of the blades from the skin.
When you have finished cutting around the bone and it is separated from the skin, if it is continuous with it, and you extract it: you should cut, and equalize its end that remains in the bone with another blade similar to the incisor. However, it is fitting that it is thin and subtle rather than other blades.
If there remains anything of the small bones, and of the sagittal bone: then take them easily, with that which is with you from the instruments. Then care for the wound after that with linen, and with the ointment which we have spoken about.
And another mode of the operation is easy, verily far from fear: which said Galen, and praised it highly: and this is his speech. And if the medicant is not stupider of men, and more ignorant of it, and is not asleep, then if there remained anything attached to the bone from the pannicle in some places, he would strike him from it with the end of the lens-shaped incisor, and free him from it with ease. Therefore, consider then, if accidents happen to the sick person which we have said, you will know that it is certainly dangerous.

Of restoration of the nose when it is broken.
You know that the nose is not broken unless both of its upper parts break at the same time, or one of them; for it has two bones: indeed, its lower part is cartilaginous, and does not break, nor does it happen to it except for wear, and wrinkling, and stretching.
If one of its two parts breaks, then you should put your little finger in the nose's hole, and align the break with your index finger and thumb, until it returns to its natural shape: and do this with ease. And so, that the pain does not happen to him by your operation,
That if the break is in the upper part of the nose, and does not reach the tip of it with a finger: then you should even out its edge, where there is little thickness.
If, however, the break is in both of its parts, then you should make it similar: and properly take care of its restoration on the first day that it broke, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh day, or tenth day by the heat of apotheosis calm.
Then introduce into the nostril a licineum made of linen cloth if it has been fractured in one part; or introduce two licineums if it has been fractured in two parts: and let the thickness of the licineum be such a quantity as to fill the nostral hole.
And some ancient restorers said, that they should be filled with licineum in butter, and should be changed every day. That is not the truth, but it seems to me that it should be filled with licineum in albinus when it is finely ground with powder for grinding. Then let go of the licineum until the hole has closed up and the cartilage has hardened.
And sometimes when a goose feather is introduced into the nose instead of licineum after it has been wrapped in a soft cloth; and this does so that the fracture in the nose is held more firmly together, and prevents the sick from being prevented from breathing.
This is not necessary. If you want to do this, then do so: and if you want to use licineum, then use licineum.
What if, while you are operating, the nose becomes hot again because of the heat of apotheosis; then fill the nose with hamamelis, or with a little oil infused with rosemary, or with a little diacetylmonosodium.
But if the heat of apotheosis does not occur, then it should be filled with a paste made of sisa flour and a thuris powder mixed with albinus. Then put on the nose a little wooden chip, and do not stick the nose with anything at all.
But if the nasal bones have been broken or crushed, then you should split them open and take out the bone with the instruments that are suitable for this purpose. Then cut off the skin, and take care of the nose when it has been ingrown, and consolidated by the paste that is suitable for this purpose, or with ointment.
If a fracture occurs between the nose and upper jaw, then it is necessary to treat the person with liniments: and administer a lead tube, until healing takes place.
The restoration of the lower jaw, when broken.
When the lower jaw is broken, and the fracture is not with the ulna: examine then if it is only a fracture of the bone, and does not break into two pieces, and treat internally: then it is easy to know.
Therefore, if the fracture is in the right part of the jaw, insert your index finger of the left hand into the hole: likewise, if the fracture is on the left side of the jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand: and push it towards the outside with ease from the inside with your other hand, and thus adjust it evenly.
If the fracture of the jaw has already separated into two pieces, then it is necessary to administer extension from both parts, according to the straightness: until the adjustment is possible.
But if it has already happened in the teeth, a movement or rupture: then tighten them with a gold wire, or silver, or silk thread. Then put over the broken jaw of the wound. Then put over the wound a doubled cloth, and put over the cloth a large piece of skin cut to fit: or a fragment of sole skin, equal in length to the jaw.
Then bind the wound from above, according to how you prepare your bindings, and the application suits him, until it is not destroyed: and instruct the sick person to use tranquillity and silence: and provide him with soft food.
But if you think that something has changed in some way: then come closer, and unravel it on the third day. Then straighten out what has been changed from it, and patch up the wound with flour ground, mixed with chalk, or with semolina, after you have removed the wound: and put over the patch wool yarn.
While that attachment remains on the wound, and the bone arrangement does not change, release it, do not separate, until healing: or hold the fracture together. For this fracture is often tightened for seven days.
If, however, a fever arises during the time of the compression, then administer to it what I have said frequently in the sedative [treatment], until that apoplexy removes itself.
And if the fracture is with the ulna, consider. Now I separate the little fragment from the bone, or from the saw: then, with this care, remove those fragments of the saw when you have it ready for removal from the instruments.

Then, when you have removed those saws, and there remains none of them, then close the wound opening if it is wide; and if not, cover it with the plasters that suit it and let it be grown in until healing:

¶ Of the restoration of the fracture of the spatula.
Rare is it that the Spatula breaks, and in its place a wide piece: and do not break from it except for the edge of it.
When therefore it breaks from that place, and it breaks in the middle of it, and is not known except by touch: then according to what its figure is, prepare the treatment of it. And reduce it over the natural form at all your skill, which is possible for you.
Then put on top of the place powder to be ground with chalk and a wooden stick, and put a flap of cloth above it. Then put on top of it an iron plate from a thin board according to the quantity of the whole Spatula, or wider one than that a little.
But if under the plate is squeezed in some place of the whole Spatula, then make that same squeeze with soft wood, until the plate comes down according to equality.
Then wipe off from above with long-haired cloth, with a constricting pressure coming down, and with the firmness of the plate, until it does not move away from its place.
And consider the ligature every day. Whenever that is softened, tighten it and make the plate equal if it moves away from its place: and lie on his sick side.
And the Spatula is restored in twenty days, or in fifteen.
When therefore that number is completed, then dissolve the ligature: and you are in safety. For that itself is of bones whose connection does not fear, and is not there of their solution.
If however it proceeds from a fragment of bone, and pierces through the skin: now cut above it, and remove it. And do as we have said about a fracture of the jaw from the heat of apoplexy, if anything happens from it.
[On the restoration of a fracture of the breastbone.]
The breastbone is sometimes fractured through and through, and this rarely happens. Its extremities are more frequently broken, and they are destroyed by the accidents which befall it.
When the middle part is broken, which turns downward, and happens to him a violent pain, and difficulty in breathing, and perhaps he spits up blood, and it appears that there is a fracture of the breastbone, and it does not hide the sense, The restoration is this: let him lie down on his back: and put between his shoulders some feathers.
Then press his shoulders together, and press his ribs from both sides with his hands: and make him even by the application of a subtle force, in accordance with all possible ways that it may be made to fit, until he has recovered his shape, according to what is necessary.
Then put over it a plaster and a board, and put under it an axle of a light table, or of similar material, and let it rest on that: for when you have bound it up with cloth. Then make him even by applying a subtle force to the bonding of it over the broken bone, that it may not move: and sometimes turn him with the binding, according to its roundness to his back, and tighten it by a tightening force.
Then consider the bonding at all times, and always tighten it when it becomes soft, and if necessity provokes to solve its problem by itching, or a corruption which happens in that place, or pain, or fever: then approach, and loosen it, and remove the plaster, and set aright what has happened in the way of curing it.
Then reduce the plaster, if you see to that face: and bind it tightly until it is healed.
The restoration of the ribs when they are broken.
You know that a rib fracture does not occur, unless in the places far from the back and from the extremities of them to the front. And they do not suffer wear and tear, except therefore, because they are cartilage. And the knowledge of this does not conceal itself from the sense in inquiry when the fingers touch.
And the restoration is done so that an equal fracture with the fingers in a second possible way, until it is even, because it is necessary to restore it. Then apply the plaster, and tightly bind the broken jaw with the strap, if it needs that.
And if the fracture of the ribs is hidden toward the inside: then a severe pain occurs, and a pricking, like that which occurred to him who had pleurisy; because the jaw stings the windpipe, and also it occurs to him difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and much blood loss: and this is a difficult cure.
And already the ancients have devised many methods for this: and among them are those who say, that you place food to generate inflammation and windiness, so as to inflame the belly, and to spread it out, and to push the fracture toward the outside.
But we dislike, so that it is not made a habit of quickly doing an emetic, if it does not happen. But if it has already happened, then add this to it.
And some of them said: Let him lie on a windy place, and then blow over it with force, and it is similar to reasoning. But it is feared that the windiness will attract superficial fluid toward the place, because of the weakness of disposition.
And some of them said: It is necessary that the place be covered with wool which has already been submerged in hot oil: and let there be placed feathers between the ribs, so that it is filled: so that the binding becomes equal when it is rolled up according to its roundness.
The sick person will recover from pleurisy with food and medicine.
If the person is forced to lie on an infirm bed, which he does not tolerate, and the nose pricks with a piercing wound, hurting the patient, and we are afraid of the sick man: then it should be that we cut him off from the place, and discover the broken ribs. Then let us place over it an instrument that preserves the siffa, whose memory precedes: and we will cut through the nose easily, and we will cut out the same one.
Then let us gather together two lips of the wound, if it is great with a suture: and let us heal him with plasters, until he heals.
If it happens during the period of feverish swelling, then let it approach, and pour oil with rose water onto the place, and put him on: and heal the sick person with it as is suitable for the swelling.
At that time he will also recover from the side where he sleeps, until he heals.
For the restoration of a broken spine of the back and neck.
For the bones of the neck sometimes there is a fracture, and it rarely happens that they are worn out: and also for the spine of the back. So when it has happened to someone, and you do not know whether he will heal or not: then trust it. For if you see his hands already softened, and stupefied, and dead: and cannot be moved, or extended, or squeezed together: and when you squeeze them with your fingers and prick them with a pin, he does not feel it, and does not feel the pain in them: then you know that he is not healed, and is mortal in many dispositions.
If he can move them and feels the constriction with your fingers and the prick: then you know that the spine of the neck is healed, and that the sick person will recover with treatment.
If it is similar to that of the spine of the back, and you do not know whether he will heal or not: then look at his feet: if you see that they are softened, and what happened to his hands has happened to them: then when he lies on his back, he moves and moves without volition: and when he rises on his belly, he moves without volition: and when he rises again on his back, and wants to drink, but cannot: then you know that it is mortal. So do not perform surgery on him.
If, however, he does not suffer from any of those conditions, the matter is less serious.
And the cure for him when he suffers from any of those conditions is such that you should bring him to a state of sedated sweat: in other words, so that you put on him only rosin-oiled cloth; or when he is bathed with warm water.
Place over it several times a day until he sweats; then place over the site one of the supporting splints, drying and tightly wrapped around him with the bandage; and instruct the sick person to rest and remain calm. And let him not lie on the injured side until he is cured of his pain;
If, however, it happens that the bone is separated from its attachment, or something that has already become detached from it; then you should remove the flesh with care and get rid of it. Then take two pieces of leather, if it is a large wound, and join them together with sutures. Then care for the person when he has an infection from the skin, with ointment, until he is cured.
If, however, the bone is finally broken at the end of the lower limb and the foot is cut off: then you should insert the thumb of the left hand into the joint, and with the right hand, according to what can be done, and by equalling it in length.
Then place over him, and remove him; and care for the wound with the cure that preceded it until he is cured.
For the restoration of ankle joints.
Chapter XI.
Rarely do the bones of the ankles break. If they break, then their fracture is not complete; they split at the ends of the bones and decline towards the inside: and a sick person experiences pain in that place, and a burning sensation: and the leg which breaks becomes numb.
And for the restoration of it, you should extend your hand over it, until you stand on the fracture as though it were a pillar.
If the fracture is only in its extremities, then make that fracture equal, according to how you are prepared, until it is similar to its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in length, or has already turned towards the insides: then make the sick one lie on his stomach, until his fracture is prepared for you to restore it. When you have made that equal, then place a plaster over it: and then place over it a wooden or leather splint, and tighten it with such a tourniquet that you do not fear the fracture's movement, nor the removal of the splint.
And with that equal concavity make it full, until it takes that form: and order the sick one to sleep on his back, or on his healthy side.
But if there should happen to be a heat in him: then cease from extending it, and from restoring it, until the heat subsides: and place over him who sits what sits, according to how it has preceded, and then return to its restoration: and tighten him as it should be.
But if it has happened in the bone's shattering, or has been broken, extract it.
For the restoration of the fracture, Assistant.
The assistant is that which is between the trough and the head of a spatula.
But if it is broken, then its restoration is according to two ways.
One of which is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form, which follows.

Then hang it from above the sick one with something heavy, so that he extends himself towards the lower. Then let the physician treat both hands together until the fracture heals, as it should.
And another way is that he lies down on his chest with his hand suspended from his neck by a bandage. Then command two servants to hold one of them up above the fracture with their hands: and let one hold below it and stretch out his hand on each side.

If then the fracture is near the edge of the shoulder, then a bandage should be tied under the collarbone, and another above the fracture towards the hip.
And likewise if the fracture is near the hip: then a bandage should be tied on the spot, and on the hip itself.
Then let the fracture be made equal with ease without effort, until when it is made equal as it should be, and applied with good application: then tie it up, if there is no swelling of heat.

And place on the person a woolen cloth soaked in vinegar and olive oil, until when the swelling is still active: then tie it up: and the way of tying it up is that you place a plaster above the fracture.

And then put the cloth on, and tie it above the aide's arm, so that the arm remains in place by the stakes: if it does not prevent you from doing so, and there is no disturbance on you from any broken bone.
That if you wish to use stakes: and it is, so that you place on the fracture a stake which is wider and stronger than the others: and let him who does this work be skilled in using the stakes. And place the width of the hand between all the stars: and let the length of the stars be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers from every side.

If then you see the length of the stars and the constriction, as we have said, at the time when you restore the limb immediately, then do it: and if you wish to let go of the swelling and the coldness, then leave off the constriction and the stars until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the binding on all three days so that there does not remain any itching or swelling, nor does food come to the limb by way of the binding, because of the swelling's tendency to burst. So straighten out all that, according to what we have said.
If then you are not confident, so that nothing comes out of it: then do not let go of the binding until after three days or more.
And let there lie on his back, with his hands on his belly, and put under the pillow a bed filled with wool, even.
Then consider at all times of night or day, so that the broken limb does not go loose, nor do the bindings become soft. So straighten out all that with your skill.
And provide for the nurse food according to the order that we have given, so that there be food of low quality at first, until the mouth begins to heal: then it should be provided, so that it grows heavier.
For the custom of the binding and the arm is that it should be bound for forty days; then therefore let it be loosed, and administered a bath, and treated with an ointment that makes it right.
If then the fracture were foul, ground: then do not let go of the binding and the stars until after fifty days, and until two months.
On restoring fractures of the brachial bone.
The arm consists of two bones called "humeri.
One is small and it is the one that follows the thumb. The other is large and it is the one placed below the little bone.
Perhaps then the larger femur alone breaks, or the smaller one, or perhaps both break at once.
When therefore the smaller femur breaks above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the smaller femur breaks below, it is a bad fracture, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse when both bones break at once.
But if the bone that breaks is the small one above, then it belongs to the physician to restore it with ease by extending the small one until it is even.
But if the broken femur is the large one: then it should be tightly bound with a strong bandage.
And it should be done so that the hand assumes a certain position in restoring it, and that its extension extends beyond the elbow joint. And let the thumb of the hand be placed higher than all the fingers: and let the flexor muscles be extended in full for each finger, or else not; and let the person sit on a square with his feet before him, or straight in front of it, in height so that he does not suffer any pain.
Then extend the arm from below, by hand, or with a bandage. And another minister should extend it from above.
Then the physician should level the bone so that he can reduce it into the best possible position.
If there are any segments broken off in the fracture, then take care to restore them to their places with your own hand.
But if it appears to you that there is a break in the bone and it pricks the skin, and there is no hope of restoring it: then find beneath it, and remove it according to the method which we have described in those preceding this.
If, however, there is a fracture with the ulna, I have already done to that chapter singularly, so take care of him from there.
If, however, there is a fracture with the ulna, I have already done to that chapter singularly, so take care of him from there. Then put on the splints, and let the splint that is put on the fracture be a bit wider, and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in various arrangements: whether it is a fracture in one lock, or in two locks simultaneously.
Then put on your splint in a stronger and more robust place at the site of the fracture, and slightly more with the splint toward the upper part of the fracture, or toward the lower part: or put on a softer splint slightly according to what precedes the memory of it at the beginning of the chapter.
And let there be cloths wrapping around the fracture, soft, wet fabrics; and not let them be hard, rough. And let there be a thread that is tied with linen thread itself, between thin and thick, as we have described.
And consider the member and ligaments after days. If anything needs to be corrected, like an itch that occurs on a member: then one should immerse the member in hot water until the itch subsides, and remove the member not restricted, until it subsides, and then repeat the splint.
If the splint is already softened, and the bone is already removed, and similar things: then correct it completely with your skill, and consider again.
For if the nutrition is prevented from reaching the member due to an excess of splint, then one should make it slightly softer, and leave it for a few days until it heals, and then tie it up.
If, however, none of those things which we have said happens to the sick person, then it is not necessary that he be healed, unless after twenty days or about that time.
Then hang his hand from his neck. And let his neck be kept straight, and let him guard with virtue his own strength from restless movements. And let him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in twenty days, or in thirty-two, and perhaps it is restored in fifty. All of this depends on the condition of his constitution, and the condition of virtue.
[Regarding the restoration of broken bones in the hand.]
Know that it is rare for a bone of the hand, or a knuckle of the fingers, to break. And it does not happen to him, unless by frequent rubbing.

Or put a splint on top of it, as we have said for the thumb.

On the restoration of a hip fracture.
The hip bone is often broken and can be recognized: because it turns towards the inner and back parts.
And the restoration of it is done so that a bandage is put above the fracture, and another bandage is put under the fracture. And the sick person extends his hand on his face.

And in the same way, if there is a fracture near the knee, then a close ligament should be tied to the knee, so that it falls on extension towards the bottom.

Of restoring the fracture of the knee joint of the leg.

Regarding the restoration of fractures of the leg.
Know that there are two bones of the leg, one is large and called "leg bone": and the other is fine and called "shaft".

If both bones break at the same time, the leg becomes deformed in all parts.
And if the fine bone breaks, the leg is deformed towards the front.
And if the large bone breaks, it injures the lower part: and it is of these which do not hide from you.
Then administer stretching, and equalization, and binding of the joints according to that being even.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a bad one, it has many things: so that there should be less stretching, and gentler relief for you.

And already let there be administered a cane from wood according to the length of the leg, and let it be placed in it so that it keeps it from moving.
And indeed it is necessary that you do this for a fracture when it is a simple fracture.

Of the fracture of the bones of the foot and the fingers.
The heel does not occur a fracture within.

On the restoration of fractures of bones, when they are joined with a fracture of the bone.

Then begin to restore the fracture in the same day: and do not put off doing it, unless there is a bruise.
But if a bruise has become hot, then do not administer the restoration until the ninth day, until it cools down. And do not approach him on the third and fourth days at all, for you would make bad accidents happen to him.
But if a bone is broken and stands out above the skin: then you should seek to reduce it and make it stand firmly with your hands, using a slight force and extension.
If, however, the extension is not prepared and his equinoctium with your hands: then reduce him with this instrument. And it is an instrument that is made of iron, the length of which is the quantity of seven fingers, or eight; and its width is according to the quantity of the wound. And for this reason it is necessary that the doctor should take from them three or four according to the quantity that he needs for the cure of any kind of fracture.
And it should be round: and it should have little thickness so that it does not double itself under pressure on it at the time of the operation: and it should have an acute extremity, which has a rim in its extremities. And it should be above its thickness by a third of its length from its lower end.
This is the shape of it.
It is necessary in this operation that its acute extremity should be placed curved on the end of the prominent bone: and push it with a single blow, until when it returns the bone and equips it with some équation.
Consider therefore to make the ends of the fracture face each other at right angles.
If its ends of the fracture are thin and does not take it with a good grip, then he should, that you hit the end of the bone, until the instrument can get a grip.

Then pare away what remains in the bone from the roughness and small bark.
But when the bone is reduced and finds itself weak after its reduction, with a painful and annoying pain, then you should know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do this then. For you have a great handicap when your restoration is complete which repairs the bones.

On the treatment of a nodulation, if there is one: and otherwise, leave it alone.
This nodulation often occurs in the process of healing a fracture. terutama that which is near joints: and it grows out of the shape of the limb. And perhaps prevents the limb from its natural function.
Consider therefore then, if the nodulation is new: administer to it medicines which have a constricting effect, such as aloe, and olive oil, and myrrh, and oak galls, and acacia, and similar ones.
So that you take some of these things, or all of them: and bind them with a bitter wine, or with white of egg, or with vinegar: and put them on the nodulation in a poultice, and tightly bind them with a good constriction.
And loosen the bandage, and do not take it off for many days. Then remove the bandage, and put on a new one, until the swelling goes down.
Or tighten with a lead strip laid斜 across. Lead is good for removing whatever fears the body.
But if the swelling has already settled into the stone, and is fixed, and the need to remove it is urgent: then find on top of it and cut off the surplus: or else saw it off with some chisels, until you remove it: and take care of it, until it heals.
As for the treatment when it is restored, and remains a limb after that, not in accordance with its natural shape.
When a bone fracture is restored, and remains a limb that is weak, then it should not be used for any great cause. Of which one is, a multiplicity of ligatures and their tightening not being in accordance with what should be: or because of the surplus stricture of the ligaments, until food running to the member is forbidden: or because of the multitude of embalmings that are superfluous at the wrong time: or because of the scarcity of blood in the body, and its weakness.
And the treatment for it is, to feed the sick and make the body light: until the blood increases in it. And apply a bath. And introduce joy and gladness to him.
Then place a piece on the limb: so that the piece attracts much food to it. And persistently apply embalmings with hot water, until food runs, and returns to its natural shape.
As for the treatment when broken bones are restored that have been twisted out of shape.
When it happens that a member is restored after its cure, and there is a prominent bone fracture or swelling: and the shape of the limb is altered: but the limb should not be forbidden from its natural function: so you need not object to its breaking again.
There were also many foolish doctors and healers who did this thing on our land.
And yet this operation is most dangerous, leading to death or great fear, as if it were mortal.
But if the claudication and fainting are recent: then it should be soaked with warm water, in which the softening herbs have been boiled, such as the leaves of aloe, and its root, and mallow, and similar ones. And let there be made for him a plaster with a softening plaster, like that of dandelion根
